Writes the “National”: “Magazine-news-
papers are tie nest to advertise in. An advertisement
which is not read docs no good, and most daily papers are
only hall read before they make way for their successors.
The paper that is read from end to end,” like The Bulle-
tin, “is the sheet-anchor of profitable advertising.” A
good, original weekly, not a hideous jumble of reprinted
stale news spiced with observations about horse-colic and
turnip-disease*, is tile best medium. Every man knows
that he skims over a daily, at breakfast or on a tram-car.
He never reads it through. But his favorite weekly is
menially laid aside for an hour of leisure, and then he
reads it, and reads it right through. The Bulletin cir-
culates everywhere in Australasia, and even sells 300
copies weekly in South Africa.

CLEVELAND STREET SYDNEY
LITERARY NOTES.
“Celt” is a label which covers a lot, yet
William and Elizabetli Sharp—jointly re-
sponsible for a new anthology of Celtic verse
—seem almost to regret that it does not
cover more. In fact, there is in the intro-
duction to “Lyra Celtica ” a hint that, if
you banish the Celt from tlie realm of poesy,
there will be hardly any realm left. It
appears that a Celtic poet is, firstly, a poet
born under Celtic skies. Or, second, he is a
poet of Celtic blood born under any skies—
and a very little blood, a very long way off,
is sufficient when the Sharps are out for
scalps to decorate the Celtic wigwam. Or,
third, a Celtic poet is anybody who cannot
be proved not to have been Celtic—Shakes-
peare, for example. Or, fourth, a Celtic poet
is anybody in whose work Mr. Sharp can find
the Celtic soul—like Robert Louis Stevenson
(a typical Scotch Lowlander). So that Mil-
ton, cockney Keats, lowland Burns, Byron,
and even Swinburne were born real Celts, in
• —O dexterous phrase ! —“ the geography of
the soul.”
♦ ♦ * *
It is no wonder, thus, although the editor’s
practice does not go quite as far as the preface-
maker’s theory, that the present volume of
500 Bvo. pages is only to be considered as a
foretaste of a big Celtic book which is coming
in a year or three. Most people will rest
content with the little one, which contains
many old favorites, and many new pieces
which as yet are only to be had in rare
volumes and limited editions. An anthology
should be good nowadays to justify itself, and
this Celtic one is good enough. The appen-
dix of biographical notes on the sampled
authors is an excellent feature. One reads
that when Gerald Griffin’s “ Eileen Aroon ”
(Nellie, my darling) came under his notice,
Tennyson “ went mooning about for days,”
repeating with endless gusto the lovely
closing lines :
Youth must with time.decay,
Eileen Aroon !
Beauty must fade away,
Eileen Aroon !
Castles are sacked in war,
Chieftains are scattered far,
Truth is a fixed star,
Eileen Aroon !
* * * *
As a flower from the Celtic bouquet, take
this little piece in modern vein :
POPPIES.
The sudden night is here at once :
The lost lamb cries and runs and stands,
For all the poppy cups are hands
To seize and take him when he runs.
The dusky cups are blood colour ;
And like a cup of blood this one
To drink, and be with Babylon,
And love and kiss the lips of her.
Thy sins as snow ! —just then it burned
The dark —a flaming face and bust;
And just beneath here in the dust
The Scarlet Woman laughed and turned.
Charles Weekes.
* * * *
Even as a poet, Coventry Patmore is little
known in Australia. As a critic, few of us
can appreciate him ; and it seems that “the
reader who desires to obtain a full view of
him as an aesthetic writer must travel back ”
to the reviews of 30 or 40 years ago. In the
March Bookman, Richard Garnett accuses
him of making the author whom he criticises
“less his quarry than his stalking-horse,”
and elevates his capacity at the expense of
his critical capacity. But there is pith in
these of Patmore’s dicta :
The follies of a Blake or a Hartley Coleridge
are venial when compared with those of the
thoughtful and prudent fool—the fool in respect of
great things, as the other is in respect of small.
It is a peculiarity of the very highest work of
every kind that it is not the result of painful
labor, but that it is easier to do it than not to do
it, when it can be done at all.
A wise or tender phrase in the mouth of a
Byron or a Moore will be despised, where a com-
monplace of morality or affection in that of a
Wordsworth or a Burns will be respected.
Originality consists simply in a man’s being
upon his own line. A society of persons of true
originality in manners would be like an oak—the
leaves of which all look alike until they are care-
fully compared, when it is found that they are all
different.
All the music of verse arises, not from infraction,
but inflection of the law of the set metre.
The real value of a large aquamarine may be
more than that of a small emerald.
In the realms of art and letters genius is, in its
initial stage, perceptive reason, the rare power of
seeing self-evident, things.
• * • •
It is not generally known that London
Truth’s clever literary critic, “ Desmond
O’Brien,” is Richard Ashe King, the Irish
author of “ The Wearing of the Green.”
* * -x * *
' In the matter of the prices which a popu-
lar English storyteller can command, the
Bookman quotes Anthony Hope’s recent re-
fusal of £l5O for one short story.
* * * *
Dr. W. Wright quotes the maxims in
which three-well known artists embodied for
him their theories of art. Said Church, the
American painter of Niagara and Damas-
cus, “ If you wish to excel in art, exaggerate
the leading feature.” Said Holman Hunt :
“Put your conscience into every leaf you
paint, and line you write.” Said Leighton :
“ Do little or do much, but do it well.”
* * * *
Lovers of Matthew Arnold may be com-
mended to Frederic Harrison’s essay in the
March Nineteenth Century, which stimu-
lates new admiration for Harrison’s critical
ability. Indeed, of all critics now writing,
he strikes one as likest to Arnold him-
self—So lucid, so impartial, so sane. “ No
poet,” he declares, “ in the roll of our
literature, unless it he Milton, has been so
essentially saturated to the very bone with
the classical genius”—as Arnold. He is
classical “ in the serene self-command, the
harmony of tone, the measured fitness, the
sw'eet reasonableness of his verse.” He is
never “incoherent, spasmodic, careless,
washy, or banal. He never fliesupinto aregion
where the sun melts his wings ; he strikes
no discords, and he never tries a mood for
which he has no gift. He has more general
insight into the intellectual world of our age,
and he sees into it more deeply and
more surely than any contemporary poet.”
(This is true. Tennyson mostly transmutes
dubious intellectual commonplace into sound-
ing harmony : he is essentially shallow.
Browning goes deeper, but his verse-stuff is
tagged witli quirks, and cranks, and thin
emotional threads : it isn’t reason - proof.
Arnold not only fits your mood, but fills and
satisfies your mind).
* * * *
“On the other hand,” says Harrison,
Matthew Arnold has “little passion, small
measure of dramatic sense, but a moderate
gift of movement or of color, and—what is
perhaps a more serious want—no sure ear for
melody and music.” The “ sure ” qualifies
into correctness a statement which would
otherwise be instantly challenged by ‘ ‘ The
Forsaken Merman” and the hymn in “Em-
pedocles,” and half-a-dozen other deliciously
rhythmical pieces. But Harrison distin-
guishes between “music ” and “modulation
and polish,” and suggests, among others,
three lines from “The Merman” itself in
support of his judgment :
—From heaths starr’d with broom,
And high rooks throw mildly
On the blanch’d sands a gloom.
* * * *
Certainly, these lines are not musical when
repeated aloud. But is it always necessary
that verse-music be considered from the
view-point of oral emphasis ? Is there
not a music of the eye as well as of the ear ?
To illustrate : an expert musician does not
need to play a score over to understand
and enjoy it. It sings to him by vision.
Possibly he might receive a truer, fuller,
better impression of it when played aloud ;
but can it be contended that poetry and
music, in this respect, stand, on the
same footing 1 Quite conceivably not. Some
minds are helped to a better understanding
of “Hamlet” by Irving’s acting ; but it is
clear that a mind more in tune with the spirit
of the play than Irving’s is—and doubtless
there are many such—might rather be ham-
pered by an interpretation of that spirit
which did not chime with its own.
* * * *
This is a delicate and devious theme, but
the writer, at all events, would be disposed
to hold that the capacity of poetry to be read
aloud is not an ultimate, or even a necessary,
test of its merit; that the poet who appeals
chiefly to the eye does not inevitably take a
lower status than he who appeals chiefly to
the ear ; and that, consequently, since there
is no finality of standard, every poet should
be regarded as a challenged duellist, and
permitted to choose, in this matter, his own
weapons, his own standard—rapier or pistol,
eye or ear. To take a local example, Victor
Daley is an exceedingly clever color-poet, but
a comparatively moderate tone-poet. His
harmonies often appeal more to eye than to
ear : his verse often loses, rather than gains,
by being read aloud. Then why insist that
reading-aloud should be a criterion of it
merit ? Every cultured person knows that
he does not consciously read letters, or even
words. The practised reader’s eye flashes to
his mind at once the picture-meaning of a
phrase, a whole sentence. Often, with a
single glance at a page, he can disinter the
buried thought, or thoughts, which are its
message. The sound of letters, or words, or
sentences, need not concern him at all. Is
it not then possible to insist too much on the
canon of vocal melody ?
» * * *
This divagation has nothing whatever to
do with some pregnant remarks by Harrison
on the general principles of criticism, which
are accordingly here quoted from the same
essay :
Criticism, according to Arnold’s practice, if not
according to his theory, had as its duty to lay
down decisive canons of cultured judgment to sift
the sound from the vicious, and to maintain the
purity of language and style. To do all this in
any masterly degree requires most copious know-
ledge, and almost encyclopaedic training in litera-
ture, a natural genius for form and tone, and
above all a temper of judicial balance. Johnson,
in the last century, Hallam, and possibly Southey,
in this century, had some such gift. Macaulay
and Carlyle had not; for they wanted genius for
form and judicial balance. Now, Arnold had
this gift in supreme degree, in a degree superior to
Johnson or to Hallam. He made far fewer mis-
takes than they did. He made very few mistakes.
The touchstone of the great critic is to make very
few mistakes, and never to be carried off his
balance by any pet aversion or pet affection of his
own, not to be biassed so much as a hair’s breadth
by any salient merit or any irritating defect, and
always to keep an eye well open to the true pro-
portion of any single book in the great world of
men and of affairs, and in the mighty realm of
general literature.
For this reason we have so very few great critics,
for the combination of vast knowledge, keen taste,
and serene judgment is rare. It is thus so hard
for any young person, for women, to become great
in criticism : the young lack the wide experience ;
women lack the cool judicial temper. It is com-
mon enough to find those who are very sensitive
to some rare charm, very acute to detect a subtle
quality, or justly severe on some seductive failure.
The ire power is to be able to apply to a compli-
cated set of qualities the nicely adjusted compen-
sations, to place a work, an author, in the right
rank, and to do this for all orders of merit, with a
sure, constant, unfailing touch—and without any
real or conspicuous mi-take.
* * * *
By the \yay, Harrison himself would come
off hardly were ‘ ‘ the capacity to be read
aloud ” insisted on as one of the distinctive
standards of good literature. The Positivist
prophet is a little, oldish, greyish man w ith
hardly voice enough for chamber-oratory, let
alone an organ-hall; and his essays are in-
comparably better read than heard.
* * * *
Again by the way, Mr. W. M‘Millan,
also a little man, and also with large ideas,
is reported to have instructed a Sydney
audience, the other day, to avoid current
literature, and adhere limpetly to the old
classics. Passing the obvious rejoinder that
the audience which adhered to the classics
would never hear Mr. M‘Millan, it is quite
clear that, if the gentleman really did say
anything like this, the gentleman is wrong.
The chief use of the old classics is as a
pleasant guide to present conduct, and
to use them to the utmost of their
old classical capacity you must have
the gloss of current literature. Matthew
Arnold, for example, would probably rank
as a classic in the M‘Millan sense of the
word, and doubtless Arnold can be enjoyed
independently of the dicta of Harrison or
other critics, but the lover of Arnold who
dips into the Nineteenth Century gets a
keener zest for his enjoyment. He finds,
maybe, his own opinion crystallised in
the inevitable words which he could not
himself command ; or he finds an an-
tagonistic opinion which sets him keenly
on the alert to examine and defend his own
opinion ; and either way he profits. Classi-
cal literature is good, but current literature
is sometimes as good, and always as necessary
to a complete education. Mr. M‘Millan’s
theory would deprive his followers of the
high privilege of contact with the best minds
of the ago ; and to what purpose ?
* * * *
Miss Badham, another Sydney lecturer of
capacity, fell into a similar error when she
exhorted a little suburban flock to steer
straight for Kipling and others, and shun the
gulf of Sarah Grand and others. Miss
Badham, like Mr. M'Millan, has her pre-
liminary paradox, for plainly she has plumbed
that gulf-—or how could she know where it
is ? And how can an accurate view of cur-
rent literature be obtained without some ex-
amination of the school of Sarah G. and her
kindred ? In truth and in fact, it is a grave
literary offence for a gentlemanly Ipse Dixit
or a lady-like Ex Cathedra to rise and attempt
to narrow the scope of the enquiring mind.
The first essential of ariy mind which is a
mind is catholicity. It gleans in every
field, and everywhere adds to its sheaf. It
cannot, without loss, pass on its task of
judgment to any other mind whatever : Ipse
and Ex may give evidence if they like ; but
from Ego must come the verdict. And a
local motto for every reader might be : No
eclecticism before survey !

BEFORE YOU
T. E A. F ;
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation m the form of a pretty show ; there is
.B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C
who says that he is the man for your money •
and so on, throughout a list as long as
Greorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water
sharks, and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the slighted, blighted North where the giant
kauris grow, 6
And the earth is bare and barren where the bush-
bee usec * to hum,
When the luok we’ve followed’s failing and our
A , . a . re out of hailing,
And it’s getting narrow sailing by the rocks of
Kingdom Come ?
ihere s a way of fighting woe, squaring store-bills
as you go,
In the trade of digging gum.
And the new-chum and the scum
. And the scouring of the slum,
And the lawyer and the doctor, and the deaf and
halt and dumb,
And the parson and the sailor, and the welsher
and the whaler,
When the world is looking glum,
Just to keep from Kingdom Come,
Take to digging kauri gum.
In the. scrubby, grubby North when the giddy sun
is set,
And the idiot-owl-cicada stops the whirring of
his drum;
When the night is growing thicker and the bottled
candles flicker,
And the damned mosquitoes bicker in a diabolic
hum,
There s a way of ending fret and of pulling down
a debt
In the task of scraping gum.
And the new-chum and the scum
And the scouring of the slum,
And the lawyer and the doctor, and the deaf and
halt and dumb,
And the parson and the sailor, and the welsher
and the whaler,
When the world is looking glum,
Just to keep from Kingdom Come,
Take to scraping kauri gum.
In the sloppy, floppy North through the dismal
winter rain,
When the man is merely muscle and the mind is
nearly numb,
When the old, old pains rheumatic fill the bones
from base to attic
And a sound of words erratic sets the pannikins
a-thrum,
There’s a way of killing Cain and an antidote to
pain
In the task of hooking gum.
And the new-chum and the scum
And the scouring of the slum,
And the lawyer and the doctor, and the deaf and
halt and dumb,
And the parson and the sailor, and the welsher
and the whaler,
When the world is looking glum,
Just to keep from Kingdom Come,
Take to hooking kauri gum.
And the man of law has gambled through another
man’s estate,
And the doctor’s special weakness at the present
time is rum,
And the parson loves the clocking on a pretty
maiden’s stocking,
And his stories (mostly shocking) scare the
neophyte new-chum.
By the smouldering ti-tree fire, when the wind is
howling higher,
They are cracking jokes that blister the Recording
Angel’s slate,
And the matters that they mention are too primi-
tive to state
At the scraping of the gum.
But the new-chum and the scum
And the scouring of the slum,
And the lawyer and the doctor, and the deaf and
halt and dumb,
And the parson and the sailor, and the welsher
and the whaler,
When the Day of Judgment’s come
O, won’t they be looking glum !
As the mighty trumpets thunder and the harps go
tinkle-tum,
And they’ve finished with the digging and they’ve
scraped the final crumb,
And the bottom’s gone for ever from the trade of
kauri gum.
Maoriland.

SATURDAY, MAY 2, 1896.
The Alleged Small Landholder.
The continually increasing lack in New
South Wales of available land within easy
reach of a market, and of such quality that
it will afford a living, or even an existence, to
the small holder, is driving the Government
to follow the example set in Maoriland and
take measures to “ burst up the big estates.”
According to Minister Carruthers, an
attempt will probably be made next session
to pass an Act whereby the Government will
be empowered to compulsorily purchase large
holdings, and re-sell them in small lots on
easy terms. In other words, instead of
“ opening up to settlement ” the remote
wastes of the Central District, where roads
and railways are few, and water scarce, and
droughts plentiful, and markets far-off and
ruinously expensive to reach, the Reid
Government has begun to realise the truth
of The Bulletin’s oft-repeated state-
ment that it is the Eastern Division
of the province which most wants open-
ing. Within the last year or so there
has sprung up in the Cornstalk province an
unusually intense desire to go on the land,
not because the land holds out any greater
promise than it used to do, but rather because
it and the Benevolent Asylum are almost the
only places left to go to, consequently G. H.
Reid and Co. promise to fill a great and
urgent want, and desefve the thanks of the
community accordingly.
* » * *
The large property-owner has become so
widely recognised as a public evil that hardly
anybody save the National Ass seriously
defends him. It is to the interest of the
community to create a great mass of indepen-
dent proprietors rather than a handful of
big landholders and beneath them a nation
of slavish, hard-worked, underpaid peasants,
and this for at least a dozen good and easily
understood reasons. The division of the
country into small holdings enables the soil
to support a larger population, therefore it
relieves the congestion in the cities and allevi-
ates the unemployed evil which is always
with us. It causes the unearned increment
—that good old phrase of which everyone is
now so weary —to be divided among a larger
number of people, therefore it tends to
create a great multitude of well-to-do agri-
culturists instead of a society with a few
bloated millionaires on the top looking for
titles in England, and beneath a vast mass
of helpless poverty. It increases the num-
ber of people who have, in the well-worn
phrase of the National Ass, a stake in the
country, and who will defend it if need be
because they have something in it that is
worth defending. Closer settlement in the
interior means more railway traffic and, con-
sequently, more revenue ; greater possibili-
ties of irrigation, because there is a larger
population to bear the expense ; a diminution
of the class which is addicted to taking its
wealth to Europe and turning absentee, and
an increase in the class which is inclined to
take root in the country ; and many other
things. And undoubtedly there is great
need to foster the small proprietary class in
New South Wales. Of the 42,000,000
acres alienated in that province up to the end
of ’94, one-half was held in areas of over
10,000 acres ; and 043 persons owned
half the private lands of the province
at an average of 32,633 acres apiece. The
small holdings (under 201 acres) amounted to
only 2,190,000 acres. In 15 years 19,300,000
acres were sold by the State, and 10,800,000
acres of this went in areas of over 10,000
acres each, while only 300,000 acres went in
small farms of 200 acres and under. In
other words, during 15 years the area held
in lots of 200 acres and under hardly in-
creased at all; that held in lots from 200 to
1000 acres increased about 40 per cent. ; the
area held in estates between 1000 and 10,000
acres more than doubled, and the space held
in areas of over 10,000 acres also more than
doubled. A province which is progressing
backwards in this fashion badly wants to do
something to its large estate-holders.
* * * *
Unfortunately the method proposed by the
Reid Government, though good in itself, is
wholly insufficient. By obtaining power to
acquire large properties and re-sell them in
small lots on easy terms, something may be
done to create a desirable class of small pro-
prietors ; the difficulty is to keep them in
existence after they are created. For many
years the State, by its land-selection laws,
has been creating an exactly similar class,
and they have kept vanishing almost as fast
as created. Many of them were dummy
selectors, whose lands were peacefully ab-
sorbed in due course by the adjacent squatter.
An enormous number were eaten up by
hanks, finance companies, and private money-
lenders, who accumulated large estates by
the foreclosure of the mortgages they held
over many small proprietors. Other estates
have been built up by systematic purchase.
The people have been going on the land in a
continual stream for a generation or more,
and they have hardly begun to arrive there
in any definite sense even now. The number
of small selectors who have gone amiss-
ing out of the New South Wales list
of land-holders is an amazing quantity.
And if the Reid Government sets
itself systematically to cut up big estates
and settle small independent agriculturists
on the land, and the bank or finance co.
which principally infests that special locality
keeps on devouring them in the good old
way, until the big estates come into existence
again in a slightly alleviated form, it only
means much labor to very little purpose.
♦ ♦ * *
The experience of the past shows that
something more is needed than merely to
subdivide the land and leave it at that.
What is wanted is a system of taxation
which will make large estates so much less
profitable than small ones that no person
with a due regard for his own interest will
want to be a large estate-holder. The
remedy, as The Bulletin has pointed out
on previous occasions with an iteration
which has made many persons feel very
weary indeed, is a graduated land-tax, only
with a steeper graduation than has yet been
attempted. If the system were applied in the
Eastern Division of New South Wales, where
the need of land for close settlement is most
urgent, and, assuming that all agricultural
holdings under, say, 400 acres were free of
tax, that all from 400 to 1000 acres paid Id.
in the £ on their capital value ; those
between 1000 and 2000 acres, 2d. ; those
between 2000 and 3000 acres, 3d! ; and
thereafter an extra Id. in the £ for each
additional 3000 acres, until a maximum of,
say, 9d. in the £ was reached, the results
would be somewhat as follows. Assuming
that the land in its untaxed state was worth
£1 per acre all round, and that the average
rental was 5 per cent, on the value, an
estate of 400 acres would remain worth £1
per acre. One of 1000 acres, by reason of
the tax, would be worth 18s. 4d. an acre
A property of 2000 acres would be worth
16s. Bd. per acre ; one of 3000 acres, 15s. ;
one of 6000 acres, 13s. 4d. ; one of 12,000
acres only 10s. ; and so on till the value of
the largest was only ss. per acre. Thus the
man who cut up a massive property and sold
it m small allotments would increase the
value by so doing, and profit thereby
while the one who swallowed up a
number of small owners and made one
vast property out of their combined home-
steads, would make his estate less valuable
the more of it he acquired. In the pastoral
districts of the centre and west the untaxed
area would have to be greatly enlarged, and
the tax would necessarily increase more
gradually, and in the urban districts the tax
would have to go by value instead of area
but the principle would remain the same in
all cases.
* * * *
Under some such policy the land would not
require to be subdivided by any forciblepro
cess of law ; it would subdivide itself. More-
over, it would remain subdivided, which is
another and quite a different thing.
system would prevent English an ° *
foreign syndicates holding estates in this
country, for a considerable area is necessaiy
to make it worth a foreign syndicate s while
to exist as a landholder, and the tax would
make that area not worth holding. It would
practically shut down on the absentee laiM-
lord industry ; only tlie very wealthy
landholder can go 'Ome to skirmish tor
a title at the Imperial Institute,* and
that person would be gradually ex-
tinguished. It would prevent banks
and finance companies bloating themselves
with hundreds of thousands of acres of un-
saleable property, to their own ultimate- un-
doing. It would greatly reduce the land-
jobbing business, for even the temporary
ownership of extensive properties would be a
costly process. If considered necessary, a
year or two’s notice might be given before
bringing such a law into operation, in order
to give the people most affected time to stand
from under, but, beyond this, there is no
reason why they should receive any considera-
tion whatever. The Retd Government has
already expressed the conviction, by its pro-
posed policy, that it is advisable, in the in-
terests of the country, to create a class of
small landholders in place of the overgrown
proprietors w r ho now own more than half the
alienated portion of the province. The Bul-
letin invites it to further consider the fact
that there is little gained by bringing that
class into existence unless it can be kept in
existence afterwards, and experience has, so
far, shown that under present conditions it
can’t be kept in existence to any extent
worth mentioning.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Exit Parkes.
The death of Henry Parkes removes a man
who, ten or fifteen years ago, was regarded as
indispensable and irreplaceable in New South
Wales politics, and who drops out after all, as
most “indispensable” men do, without leaving
any perceptible vacancy. Parkes looked for
many years like a massive historical figure.
He had the art of seeming great, yet no one
could really remember, even at the zenith of his
career, that he ever did anything in particular
to deserve his reputation. He was a master of
the science and mystery of remaining—and
that was about all. No one could boss a party
better than he did, and yet he never knew how
to use it for any great purpose, save the pur-
pose of keeping the party together. No one
could steer a party into office so adroitly as he,
yet when he had office he never knew what to do
with it, except to keep it as long as possible and
then get back to it with all convenient speed.
He never learned that the country wanted
measures more than men—even if the most con-
spicuous of the men was Parkes. Therefore
he kept the counlry waiting 25 years for the
promised system of Local Government, and left
it waiting when he died ; he kept it waiting for
Freetrade and never gave it ; he promised land
reform, financial reform, the North Shore
bridge, the land-tax, and any number of other
things which also never came. He never really
understood that the country was serious in its
desire for anything ; his idea of politics was
simply that there should be two parties, on the
English model ; that each party should be
slavishly subservient to its leader ; that the
sole business of each party was to get into
power and stay there; that the difference
between parties was simply that one wanted to
put Parkes in power, and that the other
wanted Robertson, or Stuart, or Dibbs ; that
the country existed to support these two
parties ; and that the object of legislation or
promises of legislation w'as simply to please
the country to such an extent that it
would support Parkes, or Robertson or
Stuart, or Dibbs, or some other man. In
which respect he was like the ordinary poli-
tician of his school. Under these circumstances
the advent of the Labor party in New South
VV ales politics killed Parkes politically. It
reduced his political world to chaos and threw
him hopelessly out of his bearings. Govern-
ment by Contradiction was the only art he had
ever learned, and he had learned it to per-
fection; but that system is based on the theory
that there shall always be two parties in
politics, no more and no less, and that each
shall support its leader, whether right or
wrong, and have no conscience save his The

Federation. T. Dowling’s ideas on the subject
are unique in their mild simplicity, as will be
seen by the following sample :
Nobody was more desirous of Federation than himself,
but he would not sacrifice any principles or smash up the
Constitution to attain it. He was vitally opposed to the
system of representatives at the convention being- elected,
not bv Parliament, but by the people. Federation was a
grand thing, but this was too great a price for it alto-
gether. He did not believe that under the Enabling Bill
the best men would be returned.
So far as can be judged by the debates in the
Council, T. Dowling’s attitude in this matter
is that of the 48 Rich Landlords in general. T.
Dowling, however, seems to be the first land-
lord of them all who has had the courage to
deliberately call his constituents together and
tell them what he thinks of their mental
c.ipacity ; also, he is the first landlord who has
been sufficient of a blatant ass to do so. Accord-
ing to T. Dowling, the people can’t be trusted
to elect the members of the Federal Con-
vention because they don’t know how to
choose “ the best men.” The same people
elected Dowling, therefore Dowling isn’t one
of the best men. Dowling is to be entrusted
with the selection of the Convention delegates
because he is good enough for that duty, but
the people who politically created him can’t
be allowed a voice in the matter because they
haven’t sufficient sense to be entrusted with the
work. Here the attitude gets a good deal
mixed. Either T. Dowling knows himself to
be an ancient and reliable ass, and the fact that
the people elected him is a proof that they can’t
be trusted with the power of election ; or else
T. Dowling isn’t an ass, and in that case the
fact that the people elected him shows that they
can be trusted with the aforesaid power. In
other words : The people elected T. Dowling,
and if Dowling is good enough to be trusted
with the selection of the constitution-makers,
that circumstance proves that they are
capable of selecting a good man, in which
case there is no earthly reason why they
shouldn’t make the selection themselves;
or else T. Dowling is an incapable, and
the idea of leaving the selection to
him is absurd. Anyhow, the spectacle of
Dowling, who was created by the people and
is their servant, telling the people that he
won’t allow them to have Federation, except on
his own terms, and that for T. Dowling to
allow the people (who can unmake him when
they feel that way inclined) to have a voice in
the matter is really absurd, is a beautiful
example of arrogance. The best answer to this
inflated creature, who says that it is better to
lose the chance of a united Australia than tread
on the dignity of T. Dowling, is to cast him
out bodily when he comes up for re-election
two years hence, and to do it sooner if possible,
and, at any rate, to do it with a thud that will
be heard all over Australia. T. Dowling ought
to be politically thrown out with such vim as to
enable the people at Cape York to distinctly
hear his skull crack. The Bulletin presumes
that T. Dowling has some sort of a skull.
The Newcastle Strike.
Newcastle (N.S. W.) colliers have gone out on
strike for higher wages, after an abortive con-
ference with the masters. The men pick a time
when trade is reviving, and a large number of
vessels are in the port. Their work is paid by the
piece, and is differentiated on an elaborate
scale, and the conditions vary so much in differ-
ent mines and at different times that it is ex-
tremely difficult for anyone but a local expert
to reach a general conclusion as to the rights
and wrongs of the dispute ; and the experts
contradict one another flatly. The men pro-
duce pay-sheets to show that some miners, in
some mines, at some times, have worked for a
starvation pittance; the masters are equally
ready with proof that other men in other mines
at the same times, or in the same mines at other
times, or under different conditions Jn the same
mines at the same times, have earned splendid
wages. Striking the best average obtainable,
it would appear that the industry doesn’t pay
the laborers precariously employed in it any-
thing like decent wages. That is, on the whole,
the rights of the dispute are with the men, who
are entitled to more money if they can get it.
the question whether they can get it opens up
an entirely fresh set of considerations as to the
expediency of the strike, quite apart from the
question of right. The owners say they can’t
raise wages without raising the price of coal,
and they can t raise the price of coal vilhout
driving the export trade to Japan and other
places where competition with Newcastle,
nourished by previous strikes, is already
successfully carried on. The miners con-
tradict this; and it is impossible to say
decisively which side knows most about the
matter until the experiment is tried. As the
owners have refused to try it, the miners have
gone on strike, which leaves the knotty point
exactly where it is ; and starts a third set of
considerations as to how much more trade New-
oLoro W 1 i ° se by the stoppage of supplies to
is, and the loss and vexation occasioned to
fi ® wners °f vessels whose charters are can-
celled ; and the increased impetus given to
oieign trade ; and the necessity for more hard
struggle to catch up the lost ground when the
strike is ended ; and other things. A majority

N.S. Wales Judicial Procedure.
trorbu-p Bulletin,—lf Premier Reid can in-
cat re A p pr^?Clples of the English Judi-
he wtll A i t 0 NeW South Wales'law-courts
affeeT L?° X USeful thin §- This Act will
will l 6i P rocedu re of the courts ;it
Under ir if 0 £ hange * n the substantive law.
into varm th j Su P reme Court will be divided
able to tii- S C IVISI . 011 S» an d each division will be
and Fonii.f I Co S ni J ßance of both Common Law
will bp C pnf l 6 » and the same rule of law
Law div ’ Ced U 1 the Equitable and Common
recognise di'ff m ? aSe f wliere the present courts
L a g Zfl d f ereUt rules - Briefly" where there
equity and l 01 v^“ ance between the rules of
equity and la w, the rules of equity will pre-
•i ie aw will make a distinct endeavor


m reach justice. There will not then exist the
olaspheiny-provoking spectacle of a suitor in
the Equity Court having his claim dismissed
“ with costs ” because his remedy is at Law, or
of a plaintiff at Law being restrained by an in-
junction of the Equity Judge from proceeding
with his suit at Law, because the defendant has
a good defence in Equity of which the Law Courts
will not allow him to take advantage. The
department of pleading, too, will come in for
its badly-wanted share of reform. The present
system is a survival from the middle ages.
One of its worst features is the gross ignorance
in which it leaves one or other of the parties to
a suit, as to the lines on which the case is to be
pursued or defended. A plaintiff never knows
the real defence a defendant is going to set up.
If he did, he would be able to bring out the
witnesses necessary to meet it; at present he
must bring to court everybody who knows any-
thing about the case for fear the evidence may be
wanted, and lest he should be taken by surprise
at the trial; and defendant is in the same
predicament. Add to this that the pleadings,
with their rejoinders, rebutters, demurrers,
and so on, are highly technical; so that it may,
and often does, happen that judgment is given
on them alone, without a tittle of evidence
being heard, and without any regard for the
justice or merits of the case. Under the pro-
mised Act the plaintiff will set out plainly in
paragraph form the various points of his case,
and defendant will, in like manner, state his
defence ; then suitors will have a fair chance of
getting their case to a jury, and there will be
less scope for all the frill and froth with
which the Jeremy Diddlers mask their
doubts and duplicities. This is one of the best
features of the Judicature Act, but Premier
Reid appears inclined to give Loo much weight
to the opinions of some “very high lawyers”
who are opposed to the new pleading. Of
course they are—to what reform are they not
opposed ? Besides, having grown old under a
particular system, they naturally object to any
change in it. Premier Reid says, too, that
they (“the high lawyers”) are of opinion that
the new method of pleading is open to abuse.
So is any system. The existing one is grossly
abused, and it would be a physical impossibility
for any newer method to completely do away
with the games of the stupidly mischievous or
intelligently malicious lawyers. It is, how-
ever, the duty of the Court to see that its pro-
ceedings are not abused, and this matter may
be safely left in the hands of the Judges. The
style of pleading which has been in vogue in
N.S. Wales for years in the Equity and Divorce
Courts is chiefly modelled on the Act sought to
be introduced, and, if it has proved a success
(as it admittedly has) in these jurisdictions, it
would puzzle the slippery brain of a Salomons
to say why it should not also give satisfaction
in the Common Law r Courts. The Judges will
be given ample powers—which they have not
at present —to deal with that hoary old abuse,
sham pleading; then there will be no more
“pleading to gain time,” and the pettifog-
ging lawyer will lose a large source of
revenue, and another of the blots on the admin-
istration of (alleged) justice will disappear.
Terms will be done away with, and the Court
will sit for the hearing of causes all the year
round —save, of course, during the inevitable
vacations ; and thus another license for fee-
thieving will be squelched. The Judges will
be empowered to call in skilled men, actuaries,
accountants, and other scientific persons, in
order to hear and determine causes with their
assistance. This may or may not prove
to be a valuable innovation, in proportion
as the expert is chosen for brains and integrity,
or for his social standing, but it is, at all
events, one that will find favor with suitors.
Upon these lines, or half of them, the introduc-
tion of the Judicature Act will be the greatest
legal reform initiated in,N.S. Wales for many
years. In simplifying legal procedure it will
also do something towards cheapening it. I
fear, however, that “cheap law ” is still in the
distant future: Mr. Reid is not much of a
lawyer, but he is too much of a lawyer for
that.— Shark.
The Melb. Hospital Lady Meds.
Writes a well-known Bulletin correspon-
dent :
The latest annual appointment of six resident medical
officers at Melbourne Hospital is a historic event. Two
ladies were, for the first time, included among- the first
half-dozen students of the year, and, in the natural course
of things, this promising pair claimed the privilege ac-
corded by custom to the highest graduates. Their invasion
of the ancient male monopoly was hotly opposed (also m
the natural course of things) by the monopolistic party,
and the local Arqcs was laid on to suggest all sorts of
alleged reasons why women should be denied the oppor-
tunity to gain medical experience in treating out-patients,
casualty cases, &c., at a hospital open to sufferers of both
sexes. The organ of fossildom and vested interests quoted
the foolish remarks of ignorant patients, groaning upon
hospital beds, as evidence that lady doctors are an insult
to civilisation. The prejudiced drivel of Professor Halford
was splashed in the face of the Argus’s own con-
servative Fat Man, who found the stuff very refresh-
ing. But nothing came of this pitiable demonstration
save a full exposure of its failure. The tw T o lady students
placed fourth and sixth, respectively, on the exam, list
will take up their quarters at Melbourne Hospital for
twelve months, and society at large will, to some small
degree, be benefited by the novelty. 1600 feminine cases
are admitted to the institution every year. Of these the
more enlightened minority will accept medical treatment
from one of their own sex as thankfully as they consent
to be washed by a neat-handed Phyllis, instead of plead-
ing for a hall porter to perform that service. Twenty
years hence no woman with pretensions to a sense of
. decency will detail her private ailments in a strange man s
ear, or suppose him qualified, by a bold brass-plate, to
handle her bare body and deliver her of a child. The
first effect of the female doctor in America and Europe has
been to remind the fair sex of a long-forgotten self-
respect as regards its medical treatment. Women can
no longer say that they have no choice in the sex of their
hired body,scrutineers. The day of the "dear doctor
who could identify embarrassed young matrons—not to
mention blushing maidens—by a mole on the left leg is
waning fast. Men and prostitutes will, save under excep-
tional conditions, be his only patients ere the world is a
quarter of a century older. “ Mixed ” hospitals, of course,
are doomed to become impossible. If the two sexes of
patients shall be admitted to the same institution they
will not both of them provide practice and instruction for
the sex that has run the show hitherto. The spectacle of
a mob of voung men, perspiring from expectation around
the bed of a specialty fine girl, awaiting the arrival of a
learned professor who will presently expose the dainty
morsel to view, is a hospital scandal which must soon be
numbered with prurient medical memories of the past.
Women's diseases, now so prevalent, will disappear to a
great extent, under the timely, intelligent treatment of
women doctors, consulted months, nay years, sooner than
the patient would have been able to bring herself to the
ordeal of an examination by a man. Society is deeply
interested in the multiplication of woman doctors, and
the relegation of male practitioners to their proper sphere.
„ t socl ety, being a slow-witted slave to musty prejudices
moss-grown precedents, doesn’t as yet understand
that the surgeon in petticoats is a bright promise of new
nealth and happiness to domestic life.—E.F.
Judge Murray, during a case at Sydney
Quarter Sessions, administered a well-merited
rebuke to the police for springing evidence on
an accused at the eleventh hour, without notify-
ing defending counsel that such evidence
existed and would be forthcoming. The police
practice is, in all cases, to bring forward just
so little evidence at the lower courts as will
secure a committal, and then, when the c ( ase is
before the higher tribunal, to suddenly produce
what deadly weapons they have kept up their
sleeve. Which is unfair to prisoner and an
affront to J ustice. The public, which wants every
man to have a fair trial, with a view to
securing which it cheerfully foots a big bill
every year, grows weary of the tricks and
dodgeries of the police for securing convictions
—fairly if they can, but in any way to secure
them. When Premier Reid redeems his pro-
mise of recasting the N.S. W. criminal code, the
Democratic members of the House should see
that a Public Defender is appointed. Then
and not till then—will the public get the kind
of justice it hungers for and pays for.
Politically contrast South Australia with
Queensland. Each country had a distinct pro-
gressive democratic policy before it. In S.A.,
that policy was backed by the Kingston Go-
vernment party and the Labor party, and was
opposed by the National Ass and Fat Man.
In Mongrelia, the democratic policy was backed
fully by the Labor party and partly by the
Oppositionist crowd, and was opposed tooth-
and-nail by the Government and Fat Man. In
S.A., the democratic programme triumphed ;
in Leperland, it was trodden under foot.
Now, contrast the two modes of election.
In Kanakaland, the Shark Government worked
the “plural” vote (male Fat and Property v.
male manhood), “ Bulcoeked ” the electoral
rolls, disfranchised thousands of nomadic voters,
rushed the elections a month ahead in order to
prevent other thousands of acknowledged voters
from qualifying by law, and spread the elections
over weeks in order to suit the movements of
the Government wirepullers and of plural-vot-
ing Fat and Proputty. In South Australia,
every adult elector—male and female—had a
chance to vote, and only one, and the elections
were all taken on one and the same day, thus
giving the Government no special advan-
tage. In each case, the existing Government
triumphed, contrary to the experiences of
party-Government. Thus, in S.A., Democracy
and womanhood, with fair play, won the battle ;
in Q., the Government, by foul methods, held
its own. Elections, like horses, “run in all
shapes.”
Hinglish resident critics write with contempt
of the performance—dramatically considered—
of Ambroise Thomas’s “ Hamlet ” at the Scala,
Milan. Ophelia and Gertrude are described
as simpering in garments of late nineteenth-
century cut. The Melancholy Dane resembled
a waxworks figure, and “ the acting of every-
body, from the ghost to the gravediggers, was
an offence to histrionic art.” There is no
occasion for surprise in these rough remarks.
Experience of grand opera and concert enter-
tainments teaches the ordinary, uncranky ob-
server that a talent for music and singing is
commonly associated with bad taste, extreme
personal vanity, and a marked lack of “ gump-
tion ” in the tuneful artist. People who live
by music are the least reason?,ble and most
petty-minded Of all theatrical pros., as any
manager will testify. The men make bizarre
objects of themselves in the public streets, and
take the cake for meretricious mashing.
The women have all the tantrums of
a Bernhardt with very little of Sarah’s
brain-power, and no singer, fiddler, pianist,
&c., or teacher of either sex will readily
admit merit in a rival. In short, the exponents
of music and naught else—leaving rare operatic
“stars’’out of the question—seem to have no
understanding to spare for anything else, even
when they are capable of putting a little soul
into their art, which, as often as not, they don’t.
The sweetest and most fluent warblers, males
especially, are passionless sticks upon the stage
all their lives. They learn nothing, talk
nothing, think nothing but music, music, music,
nor do they improve rapidly in that. There-
fore, when a prophet predicts an Australian
nation of singers, The Bulletin casts an anxi-
ous eye towards Italy, whose emigrating organ-
grinders wear dirty velveteen coats, and brass
rings in their grimy ears, and begins to fear for
the future.
The people who harry Sydney University
authorities to examine for the valuable LL.B.
degree, without obliging candidates to put in a
certificate of haviug attended a specified course
of lectures in the University, rub close to a
subject of genuine and general public import-
ance. Why should not all University degrees
be attainable by examination merely, the ex-
amination to be made comprehensive enough to
meet the practical needs of the various pro-
fessions ? Of course, a University must live,
and Sydney University justifies its demand
that candidates shall listen to its lectures on
the ground that it cannot at present do
without their fees; but this is making
the monetary necessities of an institution
as at present constituted the limit to a
just and honorable public demand. It is of
less general interest that Sydney University
should cease to teach than that it or some other
should assume the duty of national examiner
for all degrees attesting knowledge or con-
ferring influence. Where a man is educated
matters little. He may study law in a second-
hand bookseller’s shop, may master Greek
while a college valet, or be given lessons in
practical surgery for his services as cleaner-up
in a dissecting-room ; he may be educated by a
staff of professors or within his own. family
circle, under denominational auspices or amid
atheistic surroundings ; but if his knowledge is
wide enough and deep enough for a degree the
University of the nation should confer it on
him, without any question as to whether he ate
University dinners, paid University fees, or
played the fool at University commemorations.
Hence the agitation to throw open, as it were,
the LL.B. degree merely introduces the public
to what is a large and rapidly-growing national
requirement.
N.S. W. Registrar Henry suspended a bank-
rupt’s certificate last week because, inter alia ,
bankrupt had incurred debts without “ reason-
able or probable expectation of being able to
meet the obligations.” Bankrupt was a land-
owner, whose property had deteriorated in value,
and in his affidavit he protested that when he
incurred the debts he had substantial hopes
that land would recover its market-value in
time to enable him to meet his creditors in
full. This the Registrar ruled to be “not a
reasonable or probable expectation.” In other
words, reliance on a revival of the land-values
of a few years ago is, according to Mr.
Henry, a contravention of the Bankruptcy
Act in the same sense as would be reliance on
winning the first horse in a racing sweep.
A recent number of an English law magazine
contains an article dealing with the evils which
result when judges retain their offices, although
through age or ill-health they are really unfit
for work. A copy of this article might, with
good effect, be circulated among the occupants
of our own Supreme Court bench. N.S. Wales
has seven Judges ; but, of those, two are away
on leave, and of the remainder two are, sad to
say, in a very poor state of health. The con-
sequence is that work has become very
congested. Cases appear in the lists often for
months before they can* be heard and
disposed of. Litigants are put to enormous ex-
pense each term in collecting witnesses and pre-
paring for their cases, only to find that they
have to stand over. It is a most mistaken
sense of duty which prevents a Judge from
resigning and taking his pension when he feels
that he is really not well enough to cope with
the work. One of the Judges, now in England,
recently attempted to resume his work. He
sat for about a week, during the whole of which
time it was apparent that he was suffering great
pain. This martyrdom was quite unnecessary.
A Judge is not called upon to do the Spartan
Boy business. The public will not grudge a
pension, being more than compensated by the
advantages which accrue from the appointment
of a stronger and younger man.
John Haynes, M.P., threatens to smash some-
thing if the Reid Govt, does not give him an
opportunity of rescinding the hateful income-
tax in the course of next session or as early as
business will permit. There is a chance that
the opportunity will be afforded, for the soul of
Single-tax still breathes in the N.S.W. Pre-
mier’s parliamentary tail, but the Government
must perforce refuse its sympathy, as if income-
tax went another penny would have to be clapped
on the land, or a slice of Dibbs-Protection be
brought back. Reid and Carruthers have
already sworn off, so far as an addition to land
tax is concerned, and the whole Ministry, except
perhaps Young and Garrard, who were never
fiscally coherent, must fight to the death against
the old'* enemy. But yet many will support
Samson Haynes. There is plenty of argument
on his side, and he will be able to absolutely
sail about on a bottomless sea of mercantile
and trading sympathy. He will feel an urban
and suburban population buoying him up, but
how he will satisfactorily explain it all to his
farmer constituents is another question. “ You
may tax the farmer’s land,” said Walpole
once ; “he is a sheep, and Government can
shear him without a murmur. But the trader
in the town is a boar, and if by ineome-tax you
try to take a bristle from his back he’ll yell so
as to awake the country.”
The next politician who proposes to do some-
thing towards simplifying the law should
seriously consider the case of Rigg v. the
Standard Bank, which has dragged on for over
a year in Melbourne. Rigg and others had a
claim on the bank, and after a long, weary, ex-
pensive trial they gained a verdict. Then an
appeal was lodged, and the parties waited
about twelve months for a hearing. In the
appeal the bank pointed out that the names of
certain other people indirectly interested
should also have been inserted as plaintiffs,
and because they weren’t inserted the Full
Court held that all the time and money
and parchment were wasted, and the
proceedings must start again de novo, and
everything except the ruinous co.sts was
just as before. The omission of the names of
these other people w'asn’t even alleged to make
any difference in the facts or the evidence. The
people themselves offered to come into the case
as it stood, and abide by the decision of the
Court on the other points of appeal, instead of
going over exactly the same evidence again
from the beginning, but the law decided in the
sacred name of Costs that this couldn’t be
allowed. So plaintiff is ordered to pay his
own costs and the bank’s costs, and start the
case and the costs all over again on a technical
point, which has no bearing whatever on the
merits of the dispute.
The Old Pioneers’ luncheon in connection with
Melb. Eight-Hours festivities was a pitiful exhi-
bition of the direful effects of decay. Almost
rebels in their youth, certainly fearless and inso-
lent fighters for a great principle, these pioneers
of one little triumph have in their old age
deteriorated into a coterie of slimy grovellers at
the shrines of Respectability, Law’norder, and
Royalty. The toast of the Queen, even in
the Fattest of Tory circles, is gulped down
without comment, yet the hoary presi-
dent of the show could not permit the
swill to pass without ten minutes of ful-
some laudation of the British throne. Gov.
Brassey’s health elicited another unholy crawl,
and the remaining toasts were all more or less
wandered from to put in a good word for the
Pimperial connection or the Royal Family. The
one comic note of the whole show was struck
by one W. E. Murphy, who has just published
a book, and who took the opportunity to adver-
tise the fact m a long speech. Later on the
same Murphy bowed down before Gov.
Brassey under the exhibition dome and im-
plored his acceptance of an edition de Luxe of
the immortal work, gilt-edged and jewelled in
every hole. Altogether, the portion of the
Eight Hours show as conducted by the pioneers,
tempered by Murphy, was a disgusting spec-
tacle to every decent Democrat.
An apparent attempt to introduce the French
system of inquisition before trial was revealed
during the evidence before the coroner in the
M'Carthy murder case at Ballarat. Detective
Wardley was cross-examined as to the means
taken to get a statement from prisoner. He
explained that an information was sworn against
her for vagrancy several days after her arrest
for murder in order that she could be got out of
gaol and questioned by Detective Cawsey and
himself. The magistrate’s order was the only
justification for this proceeding. She was kept
at the detective office for two hours, while
attempts were made to get her to explain her
movements on the day of the murder. This
course was taken because there were special
orders against her being questioned in gaol.
Well might counsel say with reference to this :
“ The police seemed to have got their theory
first and fitted in the evidence afterwards.”
When the detective was asked how he justified
arresting her for vagrancy, he coolly replied :
“ She was got out of gaol that she might ex-
plain her conduct. It was an absolute necessity
to do so.” The woman was acquitted by the
coroner’s jury, but the Crown has since filed a
presentment. There used to be a branch of the
law of contempt dealing with abuses of the
process of the court. Here is surely a good
instance of such an abuse, if the law is alive
still. The means taken to procure this evidence
should prove a standing warning to the other
provinces not to follow in Maoriland’s footsteps
in extending the admissibility of statements
extracted or extorted by detectives.
The letter of Col. Bell, U.S. Consul-General
in Sydney, to the Chicago Times-Heraed,
which has been extensively quoted from and
condensed in local papers, merely expands a
well-known old Chicagoan sentiment when it
directs attention to trade possibilities with
“ countries bordering the great Pacific.” Over
a year ago, at the annual Thomas Jefferson
glorification by the Iroquois Club, Judge Shep-
hard (as reported by this very Times-Herald)
said :
For the future prosperity of this land (Yankeeland), I
would rather court the trade of Japan and China than
that of Europe. Our course of supremacy in trade tends
towards them instead of eastward. In the possibilities of
the future, I would rather have Hawaii for America than
Gibraltar.
However, Col. Bell has expanded the Chica-
goan sentiment by drawing special attention to
Australia as a happy hunting-ground for the
hog-pedlar, and the Foreign-trade press has
accorded him an effusive vote of thanks accord-
ingly. Col. Bell’s complaint is that Chicago
hams go to Hingland and are there rehandled
and transhipped to Australia as Hinglish ’ams ;
but that they don’t come to Australia direct.
This paper’s complaint is that Australia can
grow its own hams; and a similar remark
applies to the Kernel’s attempted Yankee boot
boom.
That estimable Fat Man organ, the Austra-
lian Star, starts out, in the interest of the
private trustee, to prove that a Public Trust
Office is a failure :
The N.Z. Government boast that if they possess one
department more than any other calculated to soothe a
man’s path to the tomb, and keep him jocose when the
spring flowers wave above him, that department is the
—Public Trust. All a person has to do in New Zealand is
to die —the Public Trust does the rest. You just'put a
penny into Death’s slot, and there you are ! The Trust
winds up your estate and invests the money. And you
chuckle as you lie down below fondly imagining you have
the security of the State. The Kaitangata fund is in the
hands of the Public Trustee. It was thought some of
that fund might be available for relief of the widows and
orphans made by the Brunner tragedy. JBut the Kaitan-
gata fund is sick. It now amounts to £7725 and a build-
ing let at £IOO a year. But on one of the mortgages,
amounting- to £4375, the department expect a loss of
£2OOO. So much for the security of the State and the
State’s investments !
The real fact which the Star ignores is that the
Kaitangata fund was for some time under pri-
vate management before it was handed over to
the Public Trust Office, and that it was the
private trustees who put the money of the
widows and orphans into that ruinous invest-
ment. Since the fund was in the hands of the
Public Trustee there have been no losses, and ij
there were the /rubtic revenue would have made
them ijood! If the Star didn’t know these
facts then its remarks are only ignorance and
incompetence ; if it did know them then they
are something else which need not be specified.

it surprises the very English Admiral that blue-

worth dying for.
A QUESTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“This new world we shall yet see-free from the
deadly lusts of wine and woman.”- Kf.v. Price Hughes.
Man of the saintly whine,
Man of the smug grimaces,
The worship of grateful wine,
The glamor of women’s faces —
If these, our God and our graces,
Vanish and leave no sign,
How will you fill their places,
Man of the saintly whine ?
Man of the saintly whine,
Man of the smug grimaces,
Love is a thing divine
Deeper than all the spaces.
The vine-tree the world enlace?
In this new world of thine ;
How will you fill their places,
Man of the saintly whine ?
Man of the saintly whine,
Man of the smug grimaces,
Cleanse from your heart its brine,
Quicken your crawling paces,
Linger in Love’s embraces,
Drink in the world’s sunshine;
How will you fill their places,
Man of the saintly whine ?
MACHETE.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Lyceum “ Djin Djin.”
Her Majesty’s “ In the Queen’s Name.”
Royal “ Saved from the Sea.”
Criterion “ Thoroughbred.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “Trilby.”
Royal “ The Work Girl.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Alexandra “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin.”
The second edition of “ Djin-Djin,” which
was put up last Saturday, is as bright and tuneful
as the first. It has all the old scenery and the old
people and the old legs, only the legs are set to
new dance-music, and a lot of fresh frivolities are
inserted. A song about a bicycle, and one about
an undertaker, and one about a baby that wanted
the moon and felt hurt at not getting it, and a
soft, zephyrous, soul-wail about a cottage by the
sea, where an odor of fish and old sea-weed would
naturally come in at the front door, are among
these new efforts. The general result is all that
oan be desired. In its fresh setting, “ Djin-Djin”
is practically as good as new, and should be capable
of another long run if wanted.
** * *
Miss Myra Kemble, in her time one of
the greatest public favorites this country ever pro-
duced, is to have a matinee benefit at the Lyceum
next Thursday. Miss Kemble is now disabled by
a serious and painful illness, and unless a new
generation has arisen which knows not the bright
actress of a few years ago, her benefit should be a
very large affair.
* * *
“ Saved From the Sea ” is to be staged at
Sydney Royal on Saturday. The present produc-
tion has enough glare and shock and limelight in
it for another fortnight’s run if things were in
their ordinary condition, but just now competition
is at an awful pitch in Sydney, and managers are
reaching for each other’s scalps with unwonted
ferocity, and the manager who falls behind in the
race will be promptly flattened out by the others
rushing over his trampled form. “ Saved From
the Sea” sounds attractive, anyhow. The name is
vaguely suggestive of the sea-weed, and the prawn,
and the mermaid, and the disappeared coal-ship,
and the bleached skeleton on the raft, and all that
sort of thing, and it seems like a piece that might
draw business.
** * *
The heavy and indigestible “ Broad
Arrow ” will cease to rage at Her Majesty’s this
week, and on Saturday night a four-act pro-
duction called “ In the Queen’s Name ” will make
its first Sydney appearance. On this occasion it is
expected that the British Flag will again have an
opportunity to make a few well-chosen remarks
about itself, and will rise manfully to the occasion.
One day last week the dispute between the
managements of Her Majesty’s and the Royal, re
the copyright of “ Called to Arms,” turned up
again in the law courts, and the Royal won
definitely. Therefore it is generally understood
that A. Woods and B. Holt don’t play in the same
yard now, and that Woods doesn’t allow Holt to
holler in his rain-barrel any longer.
** * *
At Sydney Cri. “ Fedora ” is also doing its
last week—in fact, nearly everything is doing its
last week just now, and Saturday will see a rush
of ne\v dramas that will be simply awful in its
intensity. “ Thoroughbred ” will be B. and B.’s
new attraction, and it is understood to be fun and
horse-farce pure and simple. Meanwhile this
1 taper drops a pearly tear over “Fedora,” which
deserved a longer run and better luck, and many
other things which it somehow didn’t get. It is a
beautifully-finished drama, very well played on
the whole, and, above all, it is true to nature. It
ends just as things almost always do end in this
ugly, mismanaged, disjointed scrag-end of a uni-
verse. So many dramatists have been at work
fixing up happy endings for their characters, or
else making them die in a state of beautiful
resignation, that it is refreshing to come across
one production where the wheels roll cheerfully
on over everybody and everything just as they do
in real life, and the anguish of a woman in a low-
necked dress won’t stop them going round worth a
cent. That kind of drama wants to be encour-
aged because of its truth, but it isn’t. Therefore,
as already mentioned, the B. and B. Co. is going
on Saturday to stage something .awfully funny
about a horse.
** * #
“Important business” having taken Harry
Rickards to Melbourne, Sydney Tivoli is at present
in a bereaved condition, but Winton, the ventrilo-
quist, and Alice Leamar are doing their best to
compensate for the loss. The suddenness of
Rickards’s departure has created an impression
in some quarters that he may possibly have gone
over to wallop somebody, or to be best man at
somebody’s wedding, or to collect a bad debt, or
to see a man about a dog ; but these are only con-
jectures. The Music Hall is going on much as
usual; if possible it is even a little more usual
than before.
** # *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus and the biggest tent
ever seen in Australia are coming by the Rotokino
from the land of Hinemoa, and open in Sydney
next Thursday (May 7th). The advt. says the
show is ten times larger than it was at its last
appearance, which fact is so overwhelming as to
make comment practically impossible.
** * *
“ The Work Girl ” continues to beard the
sweater in his den at Melbourne Royal, although
the prevailing demand for that slop-made melo-
drama is not very brisk. A Jennie Lee, or Polly
Emery, or an Emma Chambers in the name-part
might have helped the show along considerably,
but the crude methods of Miss Florrie Forde don’t
assist it much. A sense of something wanting is
wafted over the footlights by Miss Forde, even
when she heads an insurrection at the mantle-
factory, and captures a pair of pants from the
enemy. She has no acting-resources to fall back
upon in times of need, and the fat that falls to her
share is treated in a way that makes it appear
thin, whereas a more experienced stage-business
lady would fry the tit-bits in oil, so to
speak, and enhance their native greasiness.
However, these sins of omission are felt
rather than observed, and cheerful Florrie
is popular enough. “The Work Girl’s” most
palpable defect is the want of a noble young
hero with a strong right arm, who would lay down
his life for an orthodox sweetheart, if necessary.
Scot Inglis is the nearest approach to a hero, but
he becomes a weary white-haired widower after
the first act, the only object of his affections being
a long-lost daughter, and the daughter forfeits all
claim to public sympathy by falling a victim to an
empty-headed Edgar. No interest attaches to
Edgar, or the girl he has betrayed, or the father
who swears to avenge her wrongs. Plimmer
seems to be first favorite when the curtain falls,
and Plimmer, at the best, is merely a dissolute,
unshaven person who turns Queen’s evidence
because a cleaner and more respectable villain has
stabbed him in the back.
** * *
The fourth week of “Trilby” finds that
weird, attractive entertainment proceeding with
undiminished vigor. It fills the Princess’s every
evening, and supports a matinee on Saturdays.
In some respects the performance improves, in
other respects it is none the better for having
gained confidence since the first week. Two or
three of the male characters assert themselves
more than they did at the beginning, and their
extra emphasis grates upon the ear. Little Billee
is the worst of these offenders. The Bulletin
can’t blame Billee for wishing to make a splash,
but the fact that he means well in this matter
doesn’t lessen the effect of his grievous error. The
duty of some actors is to keep their histrionic
powers chained up as much as possible.
* * *• *
Uncle Tom and dear little Eva are still
discussing the rudiments of Christian faith at
Melb. Alexandra. Probably they will sing hymns
together on the old plantation for another week.
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” noivadays, is largely a
variety show, interspersed with pantomime and
slave-traffic ; there are more “ breaks ” in its dark
cloud of sorrow than formerly, and the niggers, as
a rule, appear to wax fat on ill-treatment. Dan.
Barry’s darkies are frequently rotund and seldom
cast down, or, if they get cast down, they often
manage to retaliate by upsetting the brutal over-
seer. Consequently, the boast that Britons never
will be slaves is losing much of its early force, in-
asmuch as the average slave at the Haleck would
rather bleed to death in a cotton-field than change
places with a member of Hingland’s Submerged
Tenth.
** * *
The arrival from Sydney of La Belle Maie,
the pretty little wire-dancer, is a popular event at
Melb. Opera House, where Charles Godfrey will
take an afternoon benefit next Wednesday, pre-
vious to making a farewell appearance in the even-
ing. One William Crackles is announced as the
coming comedian engaged to fill the gap.
** * *
Mesmerist Kennedy, whose instant sup-
pression would be a gain to humanity at large,
will reproduce his nasty show at Melb. Athenaeum
Hall next week, Providence permitting. Since
the Svengali-Trilby case has been cited as an ex-
cuse for Kennedy it were well to remember that
the Hebrew musician doesn’t debase his subjects
intellectually, or afflict their insides with horrible
compounds. Svengali gives them the benefit of
his genius at the cost of his life. Now, if the pon-
derous hanky-panky man, with the unpleasant
voice, would produce a miraculous singer and die,
The Bulletin might begin to think kindly of
him.
* * *
“ The German Court Singers ” are to ap-
pear at Melb. Town Hall on Thursday (30th) and
thereafter, supported by Professor Marshall Hall
in charge of the orchestra, and Madame Wieder-
mann. A question is raised as to whether the
Professor’s occasional association with show specs,
ought to be encouraged. By virtue of his £ 1000 a
year for filling the Chair of Music this intolerant
faddist has started a Conservatorium of Music,
which cuts into the trade of unsubsidised teachers,
and on the strength of their Conservatorium posi-
tion the Professor and his colleague, Madame
Wiedermann, have seemingly arranged the enter-
tainment under notice. Madame W.’s worn voice
is billed as “ honorary,” but the Professor doesn’t
publicly put in a claim to be working gratis.
** * *
When Fitzgeralds’ Circus opened at
Burnie, Tas., the other night, before a big audience,
it was announced that a difficulty in the way of
Prof. Peart doing his high-dive act was the fact
that there was but 20in. water in the tank. When
the professor was ready Dan Fitzgerald said he
would leave Peart to decide when on the platform.
There were many calls from the audience to come
down and not risk his life, but Peart said he had
never been known to come down off the platform
the same way as he went up, and as hundreds had
come many miles to witness his feat he would try
it and believed he would succeed. He emerged
smiling amid great enthusiasm, having made the
world’s record dive—sBft. in 20in. water.

The “ Trilby ” company will not appear
in any other piece, luckily for those gushing critics
who regard a capable performance of an easy
character as a striking proof of genius—in a new
actor. The “Trilby” people—notably Reuben
Fax, Morrison, and Hubert Carr—start with the
advantage of being able to realise Du Maurier’s
pictures of his literary creations. Their parts are
fitted to them. Morrison, as Gecko, is the most
striking case in point. Everyone acquainted with
the illustration in the novel accepts him as the
genuine Gecko before he opens his mouth. For
the rest, he is required to be very dazed and
reproachful, and to speak with a dramatically
“ foreign ” a-ccent. In the third act he has to
shake his wig a bit and get excited, under cir-
cumstances calculated to make him a favorite ivith
the audience. Mr. Morrison’s burst of excite-
ment is, in truth, not nearly as effective as it
might be, although it is generally praised to the
skies. He is a successful Gecko. He does very
well—but he has a very soft thing ne S T^Uby
several of the others, including v anc j her

Cockaigne: “Re the death of Boucicault’s eldest
brother, through an English railway accident. The cir
cumstances involved a strange play within a pla.v. It was
the last night of Boucicault, senior, at Drury Lane, in the
> Shaughraun,’ and, as usual, he had his manager,
Chatterton, up in arms. Boucicault meant to deliver a
claptrap speech on the Irish Question, and Chatterton
managed to foil him. Yet Chatterton had laid aside all
anger, which was merged in pity. During the first act ot
the play, he heard of the fatal accident to young Bquci-
cault, but sedulously kept it from the ears of the droll
Shaughraun and quizzical Moya—Mrs. Boucicault until
the curtain dropped on the fifth act.” „
Svengali Fax was manager Kaw in the original “Trilby
production, and understudy for Wilton Lackaye. He
got a show to play Svengali one afternoon when Beerbohin
Tree was present, and the latter admits having modelled
his Jew on Fax’s impersonation.
“Dot” Boucicault has a mummer-brother, Aubrey, in
the States, where for years he has been playing a round of
parts, from Shakespeare to comic opera. He trades more
on an attractive personal appearance than on any' great
ability as an actor, being described as superficial, careless,
and irresponsible generally.
There are three members of the “ Trilby ” co. —perhaps
the three most artistic—who, The Bulletin' hears, would
like to stay awhile in Australia : Trader, Morrison, ami
Jennie Reiffarth, the Zou Zou, Gecko, and Madame Vinard
respectively of the “ Trilby ” production. Brough might
find room for Trader, Rignold would have a good man in
Morrison, and Jennie Reiffarth would be invaluable in the
Comic Opera Co., as she has been singing in opera for
many years.
Mrs. Potter and Curly Bellew, who open their Australian
tour at Melb. Princess’s in about a month with “ As You
Like It,” bring a few ’Murkan mummers along to fill lead-
ing parts. The rest will be local—veteran Cathcart among
the number.
Jennie Reiffarth, the “mother” of the “Trilby” co.,
has played with both J. C. Williamson and Rignold in
’Murka - with the former in “Struck Oil,” and with the
latter in “ Henry V.”—l7 years ago.
The paintings shown on the Princess’s stage during the
studio scene of “Trilby'” are the work of Miss Portia
Geach, sister of Mesmerist Kennedy’s manager. This is
the lady who rode'manwise 500 miles through the Gipps-
land bush. The charcoal drawings in the studio repre-
sent some of the best known Melb. nude models, and are
the work of National Gallery students.
Melb. University students missed a point in their pro-
duction of Aristophanes’ pla.v, “The Birds,” by not
localising it and bringing it up to date with Old Bird, the
J.P. of Lormer Board notoriety, as principal funny man.
The piece would then have run for three weeks to crowded
houses.
E. D. Davies, who suicided at Esperance last week, was
about the oldest professional ventriloquist, pure and
simple, on record. He had lost a good deal of his youth
when be appeared at the earliest London music ’alls, and
was reckoned a marvel. Ventriloquism has developed
considerably since Davies entered into the business.
In London, the other day, an actress who sued a country
manager for damages sustained by being tripped on the
stage (the case was contested on the ground that the
alleged damage was only imaginary), won her suit by
showing a Rontgen photo., evidencing a displacement of
one of the small bones.
There was to be a notable performance at London
Criterion to celebrate the 20th year of Wyndham’s
tenancy. First an act of “ Money,” with the Bancrofts as
Dudley Smooth and Mrs. Franklin respectively, Beerbohm
Tree as Evelyn, and his wife as Clara Douglas. Then
“ School for Scandal,” with this great cast William
Farren, Sir Peter Teazle ; Forbes Robertson, Joseph
Surface; Wyndham, Charles Surface; H. H. Vincent
(once with “the Firm”), Oliver Surface; Cyril Maude
Sir Benjamin Backbite ; Crabtree, Tom Thorne; Teddy
Righton, Moses ; Weedon Grossmith, Snake ; Fred Kerr,
Trip ; George Alexander, Careless. Fir Harry Bumper
was to be played by William Terriss ; Sir Toby by Arthur
Bouchier; Mrs. Candour by Rose Leclerq ; Lady Sneer-
well by Miss Milward ; Maria by Mary Moore ; and, finally
the Lady Teazle was to be Mrs. Patrick Campbell. The
like of such a cast has seldom been seen before.
