Oryanist Turner will probably be running another
winter concert season at Melb. Exhibition. A trial in this
direction was made with a sacred concert on Good Friday
which drew a big audience and appeared to fill a want.
Some people complain that “ Trilby,” on the boards, is
marker than “ Trilby ”in print. And so it is, of course.
If the play were not made of stronger stuff than the book
in some places the public wouldn’t be fetched It is con-
centrated essence of the book, with a little black pepper
added. . ((mI
Avrovos the B. and B. production of “The Importance
of Being Earnest,” critic William Archer calls the play
« an iridescent filament of fantasy, grateful and comfort-
jn,r ” it “imitates nothing, represents nothing, means
nothing except a sort of rondo capriccioso, in which the
artist’s fingers runs with crisp irresponsibility up and
down the keyboard of life.”
Anthony Hope’s dramatised story, “The Prisoner of
Zenda ” which “ the Firm ” has secured for Australia, is
at present drawing £2OOO a week at London St. James’s.
A special co. will be imported to play it and other pieces
(including the long-deferred “ Royal Divorce ”) here.
Pro. : “ I was one of the audience up-country (N.S.W.)
when ‘The Shaughraun ’ was played with six people.
One man essayed five parts, and only objected to his work
when, in .one important scene, two of his impersonations
had to converse. The same night the leading |&dy took
ill half-way through, and the last act was played without
a Clare Ffolliot. Yet “bush-whackers” wonder why
they have to walk back to Sydney.
Pro. : “ Apropos your remarks re pirate plays : from
half-a-dozen men in Sydney I can get “scrips” of
nearly every play produced in London, New York or the
colonies. That is, of course, plays of any value. It
would pay the holders of rights to have a detective watch
some of the travelling co.’s. Many of these barn-storm-
ing troupes simply alter the name of a play, others don’t
even take that trouble. And the murderings of the
pieces—Ugh ! ”
A recent Sketch devotes a page and a-half to an inter-
view with J. C. Williamson, and a column notice of Harry
Musgrove. J. C. says “ the Firm ” has made most money
out of comic opera.
A Brough and a Boucicault, both of them known to
Australians, are appearing (or were) in “The Romance of
a Shopwalker,” at London Vaudeville. Sydney Brough
and pretty Nina Boucicault.
Fergus Hume’s farcical comedy, “ The Fool of the
Family,” ran for about the shortest time on record—two
consecutive nights. Then it was “reluctantly with-
drawn,” and handed hack to the author along with the
management’s deepest curse.
Max O’Rell has blossomed as a playwright, a farcical
comedy of his called “John Bull on the Continong”
having been played in Canada.
A composer attributes his recent insolvency to “an
alteration in public taste in favor of a different kind of
musical composition from that with which my name is
associated.”
lii one respect the S.A. general election
just concluded was very different from its prede-
cessors. The last Parliament amended the elec-
toral law by making it an illegal practice to publish
posters, circulars, or advts. attacking the charac-
ters of candidates without the name of the person
authorising such publication being attached. This
has resulted in the abolition of one of the brightest
features of the old election days, when it was cus-
tomary to circulate at the eleventh hour posters
suggesting that candidate Smith had been living
in adultery with another man’s wife, and asking
the electors if such conduct was worthy of a local-
preacher ? Another wholesome amendment of the
law is the provision for a statement by candidates
of their election expenses—now limited to <£loo
for every 2000 electors on the roll.
The death of ex-Premier Parkes arose
from heart-failure following on pneumonia. For
several days the medical men in attendance on
the veteran had considered his case well-nigh hope-
less, owing to a recurrence of the cardiac weakness
which so markedly showed itself when he broke
his leg some years ago. The boi>des,sly-improvi-
dent old man made no will, having nothing to
dispose of—there were but a few shillings in the
house when he died. The only legacy he will leave
his wife are six children, still young, by a former
marriage. There can be no doubt that provision for
the maintenance and education of these children
will be made by Parliament, failing immediate ade-
quate private action in the matter; for Parkes
so long, with the consent of the people, occupied
the position of head of the State in N.S. Wales
that it would be ignoble and even disgraceful to
leave his children to the uncertain care of inter-
mittent charity. The Bulletin hopes to see a
resolution on the subject submitted by Govern-
ment at the opening of Parliament.
That excised portion of Bird, ex-J.P.’s
published letter to the Vic. Solicitor-General was
as follows :
And now I must refer to a matter I wish I could leave
unsaid. Judge Williams should first have pulled that
beam out of his judicial optic and then he would have
seen clearly to pull the motes —and they were only motes,
for from faults there are none of us free —out of my, then,
magisterial peepers. As soon as I mentioned that I
hailed from Cork, where the honorable and learned Chief
Justice also hails from, it had the same effect on the
Judge (Williams) as holding up a red rag to a bull in a
paddock has. His demeanor towards me instantly
changed ; he became petulant, and when opportunity
offered exhibited a bias against me. A Judge who did
write such a fiercely malevolent letter in the Argus of 9th
as Judge Williams did against the learned
f I have no faith in until he practises some
of that moral courage which he preaches in what Manning
calls the “ garish day bright ruling his wile,” bv publicly
withdrawing that letter and apologising fpr having
written it. Although the season is Easter-tide, immola-
tion of a Cork honorary magistrate upon the altar of his
country can never atone for the wrong done to a Cork
Chief Justice, for two wrongs never did nor never will
make one right.—l am, &c., George Bird.
Lovely idea : the two gintlemen from Cork!
Patriotism, the last refuge of a bowled-out J.P.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The balance-sheet of the Colonial Bank (Mel-
bourne) is about the most disappointing document of its
kind issued since ’93. It sets forth that the net profits for
the half-year are £2564, as compared with £7BO for last
half-year (when the reduction of interest was only par-
tially effected), with a loss of £4694 for the half ended
March, ’95 (when the 4J, per cent, rate still prevailed), and
a profit of £2246 for the half ended September, ’94. The
situation, consequently, pans out as follows : The Colonial
has £1,597,354 of deferred deposits. The interest on these
has just been reduced from 4}4 to 2U per cent., which
means a saving of £15,973 for the halbyear. Yet, com-
pared with the results for the last half-vear at the old
rate, the concern is only £7258 better oft, and £2300 of
this is gained by cutting down working-expenses;
so, by its own showing, the Colonial’s business has
depreciated so much that over £B7OO of the saving
made off the reconstructed depositors has already van-
ished, and onlj r hard economy kept the vanishment from
being £ll,OOO. Six months more of deterioration at
the same rate would leave the institution with another
big loss to faoe, despite the reduction of deposit-interest.
It is to be hoped that bottom has been reached ?
The Colonial Bank’s old deposit money now
amounts altogether to £1,901,398. Of this £304,044 has
been converted into preference shares which at present
receive no dividend ; the balance carries 2| per cent., so
the rate all round is only £2 2s. 2d. per cent. Yet even
though it gets the bulk of its funds at this amazingly low
rate the Colonial does little more than pay working ex-
penses ; its profits only represent 12s. and an odd copper
or two per cent per annum. The Colonial, like two other
banks, is waiting for an amendment of the Companies
Act to enable it to write off lost capital and
formally reveal to the public its actual position. If a
bank that has written off its losses and got
into a sound position should pay 5 per cent, dividends—
which doesn’t seem unreasonable when the bulk of its
deposits only cost 2£ per cent. —and the Colonial proposes
to get to this destination, it will have to write off, on the
present basis of its affairs, £740,000—a1l its paid-up
ordinary capital and two-thirds of the preference capital.
The current business seems to have somewhat improved
and the available cash (£506,000) is equal to 21 per cent,
of the liabilities to the public, which is a considerable
improvement on previous figures. Otherwise affairs look
none too rosy.
* • * *
A Bairnsdale (Vio.) farmer has netted over £75
out of three acres of small black (Russian) sunflower. The
seed sells at 3J4d. per lb. for oil-producing purposes.
* * * »
Commercial Bank of Aus. preference shares
(£lO paid) sold last week at 64s—a rise of £1 inside a month.
Colonial preference (£9 15s. paid) were at 715., whereas last
Dec. they were 375. There is nothing in the balance-sheets
to account for either movement, so evidently the “ resto-
ration of confidence ” is doing it.
* * * *
The Riverina Frozen * Meat Co., a Melbourne
concern now only in its second year, shows a profit of
£1420, equal to over 7 per cent, on its small capital of
£19,551. This is after paying £383 preliminary expenses,
carrying £l5OO to a reserve for depreciation, and paying
£1293 Interest on a £lO,OOO overdraft, and £15,000 worth
of debentures. The Riverina F.M. Co. is staggering
along under the burden of a great deal too much bor-
rowed money in proportion to capital, and it needs to go
very slow indeed in the matter of dividends until this
burden is lifted. If it does this its prospects look first-
rate.
* * * *
The first Vic. beet sugar co. is being formed at
Maffra. It is to treat 30,000 to 40,000 tons of beet per
annum, which should mean 4000 to 5000 tons of sugar.
This will supply one-tenth of the local consumption, so it
is at least a good beginning for the new industry. The
State assists the enterprise with a loan of £50,000 at 4
per cent., repayable by instalments spread over 24 years,
and the infuriated importer calls this aid “coddling” and
“ grandmotherly legislation,” and other unpleasant names
which don’t matter to anybody.
* * * •
Two new English banks — the Bank of Western
Australia and the London and Western Australian Bank
—have been registered, with a view to the further ex-
ploitation of this suffering country. Australia was badly
over-banked a little while ago, but if the Mercantile,
Federal, and City of Melb. have only dropped out to make
room for some more foreign institutions, the result is a
loss instead of a gain. An absentee-tax which will strike
the absentee bank along with the rest is much needed.
* * * *
The latest statement of accounts re the Cheviot
estate, that allegedly disastrous speculation of the Maori-
land-Govt., shows thatthe amount which the State has in-
vested in the property is now £274,000, and the rents from
small settlers are £13,379 a year, equal to a little over
£4 17s. per cent, per annum. As the Govt, raised the
purchase money at about £4 6s. per cent, the result shows
that the Seddon Ministry has its head distinctly screwed
on the right way.
* * * »
Tooth’s Brewery Co. (Sydney) has made £44,568
out of beer during the last six months. It pays a div. at
the rate of 7 per cent, per annum (£31,500) ; adds £6OOO
to reserve, making it £60,000;. £2OOO to depreciation
account; and adds the rest to the balance carried for-
ward, making it £8614. What is the good of preaching
the great Cold Tea gospel in the face of such figures ?
* * * *
The reconstruction scheme of Carlton and West
End Brewery, Limited (Melb.), doesn’t look like eventuat-
ing. To an outsider, any scheme for converting unsale-
able call-bearing shares into new shares with a lighter
sword of Damocles attached, would seem to be a boon,
especially at a time when the ordinary speculator is
deafened with calls. Certain ungrateful shareholders,
however, spurn the benefits sought to be shed upon them.
Meanwhile, certain institutions interested to £750,000
are praying that it may please Providence to paralyse or
“ take home” the arch-objector, and that at the earliest
possible moment.
Speaking of brewers and brewing in Victoria.
Out of every one of the many breweries that have gone
bung as limited co.’s or are in a dubious condition, the
original proprietors made either competencies or hand-
some fortunes. “ Limited ”in re breweries seems to indi-
cate the length of career. At the present moment, there
are not more than two actually prosperous Ltd. breweries
—of any importance—in Melb., but it is noteworthy that
several small concerns, making a specialty of sound,
drinkable beer, are gradually getting good foothold. This
could not have been possible five or six years ago, for the
reason that all the big Ld. breweries were permitted by
the banks to advance almost anything they liked on any-
thing the breweries chose : hence the competition among
bank-supported breweries to secure “ tied ” houses not
only inflated the values of such properties inordinately,
but shut out the little men whose only merit was good
beer. It only required two or three years’ industrial de-
psession to convince the “ tied ” customers that they had
paid ruinous premiums for the title of landlord. The
result is the “ writing-off” of millions by Melb. breweries.
Ine writer predicts that “ lease, license and good-will ”
will cease to have a commercial value within the next five
years, except in cases where the position of the hotel is in
itself a big thing.
Some 570 respectable individuals, who had a bit
to invest in the “ good old boom ” days, were reminded by
Justice Holroyd last week of the evils of drink, especially
beer. lor this admonition his Honor charged them ss.
.? r .i? s^'are . they held in “The Victoria Brewing,
Malting and Distilling Co. Ltd. (in Liquidation).” The
expense of writing and printing the name of the co. alone
would ruin any concern outside Rothschilds.
Aitkcin, the original proprietor, made a pile ; the
Nations, his successors, also did well at it, and sold the
concern to abovenamed limited co., £255,000 capital. A
short career and the co’s bankers sat down heavily.
Some 12 months ago the co’s late bankers sold the con-
cern in London, taking part cash and part debentures.
The London co. proposed to knock sheol out of all the
other local breweries (lager and tanglefoot) by erecting
new machinery and adding largely to the premises. They
also planked down a good stiff sum, but a hitch arises
now.
* * * *
The City of Melbourne Bank enquiry hasn’t
thus far revealed any purpose beyond legal expenses.
* * * *
This present resurrection of Jimmy Mirams and
his building society dealings ought to be considered
together with a certain abortive scheme for getting
Mirams whitewashed by the Vic. Assembly at the moment
when a prosecution of Matthew Davies was impending.
Several friends of Mirams and Davies—as The Bulletin
doesn’t forget—were anxious to pose him in a martyr
a-ttitude before the J. B. Patterson Parliament. The
libel-action now raging in Melb. County Court will show
the exact amount of sympathy due to Mirams, whose
“ barrackers ” of three years ago included James Service
and Alfred Deakin. Therefore, the political honesty of
Service and Deakin is almost as much upon its trial as the
Argus, seeing that the newspaper denies the possibility
of intelligent belief in Mirams’s cause. .
* * * *
Amusing to compare reports of Mirams v. the
Argus in the two Melbourne morning journals. The Age,
as of yore, shows no enthusiasm for revelations touching
the cruel fate of the Premier Building Association’s funds.
It doesn’t mention that a certain newspaper proprietor
was one of the original trustees, and it omitted to print
Puryes’s exhaustive narrative of the scandalous land jobs
carried through by a relative of that newspaper man’s
and the precious Murphy family.
* * * »
The Govt. re-valuation of station properties in
north-western N.S. Wales has resulted in a reduction of
rent of something like 50 per cent. If the re-valuation in
other parts of the province comes out on anything like
the same basis it will mean that there has been a pretty
general fall of at least 25 per cent. Supposing this to
happen, and it seems quite probable, will certain banks
which hold enormous areas of squatting property keep
them standing in their books at the old figures, or write
them down to present values? If they do the latter,
what will happen to them now? And if they don’t, what
may happen to them later on ?
* * * *
Illawarra S.S. co. pays a half-year’s dividend at
the rate of about 8) per cent, per annum. United Insur-
ance co. pays a half-year’s div. at the rate of 12£ per cent.
* * * *
One peculiar feature of the Sydney boot-trade
dispute. Masters usually prefer piece w ork as a guarantee
that a full equivalent for wages paid is obtained, and
object to time-payments because money may pass for
work not done. In this instance it is the men that prefer
piece-work —evidently because a fair return for work done
is larger than what the masters wish to pay per day for
the same amount of work.
♦ * * *
Hearken to a summary of “a representative
colliery proprietor’s ” views of the situation in the New-
castle (N.S.W.) coal-trade strike : “ Strike can’t last; men
lack resources ; not been provident in the past good
lor ! what had they to be provident with ? —“ A.M.A.
virtually wichout funds ; public sympathy absent; other
trade-unions can’t give sufficient aid ; and every effort
would be made to prevent the granting of credit tr the
strikers.” ! ! ! The law of human existence : If no money,
no food, no credit, and no livable work, then either Go-
vernment must feed, or food must be got by force.
* * * *
N.S.W. Govt, is moving to re purchase lands
from pastoralists in order to offer them for agri-
cultural settlement. Ashton, M.P., has suggested
that the values stated by squatters in their land-tax
returns should be taken as forming the basis of such re-
purchases. And why not ? What other possible basis can
a logical Government take ? The Bulletin said long ago
that the State should reserve the right of purchase at,
say, 19 per cent, over the owner’s valuation. With that
provision the land-tax would yield a lot more money than
it does, and resumption would be about 80 per cent,
cheaper.
* * * *
At Drake (N.S.W.) Hynes, a butcher, was
owner of an hotel erected on ground held by business
license and rented to a tenant who refused to pay his rent
and eventually jumped the ground on the plea that a
business license was invalid unless the holder lived on the
ground. Warden Steele decided in favor of Hynes, but
notice of appeal has been given.

New Bushman’s, Parkes (N.S.W.) —645 tons
4050 z. What excuse this time ?
Lone Hand, Bulgandra (N.S.W.) :—“ Since the
mine was opened up 120 tons stone have yielded 4000 z.,
the average being well maintained throughout.” The
fact that the mine has been opened up nearly two years is
not worth mentioning. It is the omissions in N.S.W.
mining that make the writer feel tired.
A party of miners have found a good copper-
lode near Blayney and are sending away 100 tons of high-
grade ore. It is on private property. The N.S.W.
Mining on Private Property Act, almost unworkable in
any direction, does not apply to copper at all. These men
are burdened with a 10 per cent, royalty.

That Melb. band of 18 speculators are putting
up N. Brokens. They are the only people who can crawl
in out of the wet; the public can’t.
* * * *
Conley and Barrass have banked 3600 z. gold
for the week’s run from their splendid show at the Peak,
Cobar. Up to now the mine looks a 2 to 3oz. thing, with
endless dirt to draw upon.
* * * •
The mess made at Wyalong by the Mines Dept,
will, it is hoped, never be repeated in N.S.W. Wyalong
was proclaimed a miners’-right field. No leases were
allowed, consequently the place is pegged out in small
areas, averaging about 200 ft. on the line of reef. One
claim measures 17ft. The Dept, allows claim-
holders to change their claims to leases, but not
to take in any additional area whether available
or not and it does not permit of amalgamation of claims
with that view. At Wyalong bigger blocks are as neces-
sary as better titles, and the latter have been given
without the former. The articles recently appearing in
Sydney D.T. give the best description of the field that has
yet appeared. They indicate that in many cases the
veins from which splendid returns have been made will
pass out of these narrow claims at comparatively
shallow depths, and the side-on ground, because of
the early bungling on the part of the Dept., is not
obtainable. Wyalong returns are as follows: Battery
treatment, 7895 tons, 19,4320 z.; average per ton, 2oz.
9J4dwt. Chlorination (ore), 755 tons ; average per ton,
soz. 14>/>dwt. Chlorination (concentrates), 155 tons, 960
oz. ; average per ton, nearly 6oz. 4dwt.
When Wyalong started, nobody knew whether
the gold would “go down ” into the rock, or whether it
would end with the soft surface country. Most of the
experts held that the mullock veins wouldn’t live into
reefs. Consequently, there was a general outcry that the
field should be kept for “ working miners”—and it was.
Consequence : instead of being divided into a dozen big
leases, resting on exemptions and trying to float them-
selves into £lOO,OOO co’s in London, the field has been cut
into 100 profitable little claims, the profits are well divided,
and a big population has been nourished from the start.
Now that it is found many of the reefs improve with
depth, the obvious plan is for adjoining claims to amalga-
mate into leases, take up vacant land alongside, and get
capital to do the sinking on the bettered security. Why
won’t the Mines Dept, permit amalgamation ?

• » * *
The old Copper Blow, known as the Silver
Hill, has been re-floated —on the strength apparently of
past failures, like a good many others.
* » * *
Junction North shares have gone steadily up-
ward on the reports of a good width of ore of first-class
quality. These reports are true. The ore is wide and it
is good. But its length is as yet undetermined—in other
words, it has not yet been shown that the lode does not
pass into the North (perhaps even into the extreme north-
west corner of the British).
* * » *
Whispered that the B.H. Proprietary has
acquired a large area of ground on its eastern boundary.
* * * *
Re the proposal of a European co. to take over the
B.H. British mine and smelting-plant—the latter a very
costly piece of machinery—it is just as well to remember
a report called for by Govt, from the Board of Health ex-
perts on lead-poisoning. Dr. Ashburton Thompson, the
officer despatched to make the report, recommended that,
although it was not advisable to insist upon the furnaces
in blast being provided with fume-condensing appliances,
no idle plant should be permitted to restart without such
appliances. The British has been an idle plant for some
time. Meantime, the improvement in the appearance of
the workings continues, though most work is being done
on shallow depths—on the 200 and 100 levels. Along with
the sulphides, the development of carbonates proceeds :
a big dump of the latter is being made at surface. The
concentrating-plant, in the erection of which there has
been exceptional expedition, is now being finished.
* * * *
The B.H. Consols to be reconstructed with a
capital of £600,000 ! The subject is too intense for words.
* * * *
Good coal has been found 103 ft. down at
Queensferry, Western Port Bay (Vic.). The co. has 3700
acres, and reckons that there are 22 different outcrops of
coal on the surface of its ground. If the new discovery
proves as big a thing as is imagined, the co., with cheap
water-carriage at its front door, should have a big thing
in hand —if it has sense enough to hang on to it, instead
of selling out to the omnivorous Bull.

and N. Cornish, Daylesford, into an £BO,OOO co. A pair of
plump div.-payers in the past, the North tallying £290,000
odd on £4OOO capital. Usual story —when the reef pinched,
there was no cash to prospect the deeper levels.

Clarence, Eaglehawk’s 6800 oz. yielder and
£lO,OOO div.-payer in ’94, seems on the eve of revival.
Trial from the east leg of the reef, cut in the 980 ft. level
crosscut, averaged about 12dwts., and 43ft. above the

mine drained shareholders of £3OOO in ’95.
* * * *
Magdala-cwm-Moonlight, Stawell (Vic.), fort-
night’s milling, 587 tons for 2510 z. 19dwt. Mainly stone
from the 1405 ft. level. Maintains this steady average all
the year round. No wonder adjoining co’s are breaking
their necks to get this lode. North Magdala seems to
have got it, and another nine weeks should bring it on
the reef. The core from the reef was 18ft. 9in.

early.

Rutherglen (Vic.) is gradually coming on.
Three months’ figures of the principal mines : Chiltern
Valley, 23480 z. ldwt. ; Prentice Freehold, 22100 z.; Great

* * * *
The erratic Collman and Tacchi looks in for its
annual div. turn. Prospecting at the 1645 ft. level has
exposed a 7ft. body of payable stone, 232 loads milling
1490 z. 9dwt. The lode has a permanent appearance, but
then the Collman has so often looked well and gone back
on its backers. Last half the co. paid one div. (£675),
later on asked for it back, and also borrowed £5OO from
Lansell to avoid making another call.
* * * *
“ As rich specimens as were ever before taken
from the mine have been got from the green lode at the
Croesus.” Perhaps that is why the battery is hung up ;
the gold is so thick it can be more quickly extracted by
hand.
* * * *
Burning rivalry exists between Coolgardie and
Kalgoorlie re the respective claims of each to the proposed
Menzies railway. The Coolgardie route appears the better,
but Kalgoorlie is suffering from a swelled head. Should
poor Coolgardie lose the Menzies line, there seems to be
nothing for her but to sink to the level of Southern Cross.
* * * *
Even the mouldy West Australian has begun
to remark the significant fact that at present there is no
div.-paying mine about Coolgardie^
Hannan’s, despite the Boulder, is not the para-
dise it claims to be. As a matter of fact, Hannan’s is a
miserable, dusty, one-horse town living on the relics of
last year’s boom, and praying for another boom to save it.
A claim on the Mt. Burgess road with 600 tons
of golden stone at grass, freely reported by the manage-
ment to go soz. to the ton, has been under offer several
times for £BOOO. And yet they wonder why it doesn’t
sell.
* » * *
There has been fierce excitement in Perth (W. A.)
over the reported gold-find in the Darling Ranges, 50
miles distant. As soon as the field began to attract
general attention the ex-editor of a Perth paper, after a brief
inspection, pronounced it a duffer, and alleged that there
was no stone in the district like that shown as coming
from the field. The inference was that persons interested
were trying to work a swindle. The ex-editor soon dis-
covered that he had stirred up a wasp’s nest. The spon-
sors of the field immediately set forth to prove its genuine-
ness. This they did to the satisfaction of the Perth share-
broking-boom people, who loudly called for the head of
the ex-editor on a charger, forgetful of the great ad. he
had given the field. After the boomers had raged for
some days the field was hall-marked, according to the
accepted traditions of Westralia, by the Mayor of Perth
“ receiving ” the discoverers at the Town Hall, whereat
the great men of the Stock Exchange positively proved its
genuineness by drinking its health and the health of its
discoverers and a lot of other healths in champagne.
After which it is time to look for crushings. A good
feature is that there is no talk of “ jewellers’ shops.”
Plenty of stone is promised, and the grade is described in
dwts. The situation of the field is favorable for cheap
working, and a very few dwts., it is claimed, will pay.
T. J. Oashman wires The Bulletin : “Have
been month Dandalup, Darling Range; examined it
thoroughly. No trace of gold in reef. Consider it a rank
duffer, and the greatest wild-cat I have seen in 26 years’
experience.”
* * * »
The Bulletin has by mail the just - issued
London prospectus of a W.A. mine—capital over £lOO,OOO
—and notices that the show has been reported on by three
people (two ex-Sydneyites and a Melbourne man), two of
whom, to this paper’s certain knowledge, never saw a
mine in their lives (barring potato-mines) until within —to
give a liberal margin—the last 14 months. And yet, so
far as is disclosed by the prospectus, their reports are the
chief reasons adduced to J. Bullion for buying the alleged
show. Knowing those people as this paper does, theii
appearance as mining experts is as ludicrous as would be
the sudden appearance of poor old Paddy the Ram as
coiffeur in a fashionable ladies’ barbery. The smellfulness
of that Westralian boom !
* * * *
Letters to hand from Day Dawn, Cue, and other
places on the Murchison ; from Coolgardie, Hannan’s,
White Feather, and other parts of the Coolgardie fields ;
also from Dundas and the Norseman, all telling the same
story, and all warning men not to come there for work.
If The Bulletin published the letters received every
week in support of the stand it has taken re W.A. boom-
mining, it would have no room for anything else.
*** ' *
Menzies, W.A., has started with two very fair
crushings—Crusoe, 130 tons, 5620 z. ; Friday, 100 tons,
2400 z. Big things have been said of Menzies by big men.
W. R. Wilson, one of the gamest of mining men, swears
by it. The veins are in a formation distinct from Cool-
gardie’s.
* * * *
Bayley’s No. 1 South —140 tons, 2720 z. It has
a big lode in which there are occasional rich pockets. It
has never paid a div., and its two years’ working and
crushing proves it a very low grade concern altogether.
Reputable Barrier manager’s terse comment on
the Junction Consolidated, Adelaide’s latest B.H. float:
“ Not even position to recommend it.”
* » * »
Croesus Extended. — One paper said .£lO,OOO
deposit had been paid, and that the purchase price was
£26,000 as well as shares; another, that it was £450
deposit. It now transpires that the mine has been placed
under offer till the end of June, and the cash considera-
tion, if the deal comes off, is £3500. The exact amount of
the deposit is still generally unknown. Ten days ago the
shares were Is. 3d. ; they, then, on the strength of fairy-
tales about the sale, jumped to 4s. 6d., and afterwards re-
ceded to 2s. 6d. No comment is necessary.
Great assistance is lent to the wild-cat promoter
by the Westralian system of mining registration, every
lease being given a new number when a transfer is
lodged. Thus, when Gilligan’s Reward South is re-
christened the Rat-Catcher by a new gang the Eastern
and English public have absolutely no means whereby it
can be identified as the magnificent swindle which went
20cwt. road-metal to the ton and so effectually cleaned out
investors. Even those on the field experience the
greatest difficulty in “ placing ” distant blocks, hearsay
being, as a general rule, more reliable than official evi-
dence. The same patch of valueless earth is consequently
sold and resold, perhaps to the same shareholders, without
anybody suspecting that the lease 224,570, over which
they blewed their good gold six months before, is identical
with lease 56,278 now being floated for £150,000 in London.
* * * *
Britishers have practically diddled the Adelaide
people out of the Lake View. Before mooting the pro-
posal to refloat the co. in London for £250,000 they quietly
mopped up 60,000 out of the 80,000 issue, and could thus
dictate. When the Adelaide directors were inclined to
“ buck ” at the game, the syndicate had the steeled cheek
to tell them, “We have a much higher opinion of the
mine than you have, and we consider that instead of
scratching the surface as you have been doing, you should
develop it, and for that you want better heads and more
capital.” The grabbers’ troubles in developing it. They
are after a big “ cut ” out of the new float. Proposed co.
will have £30,000 working capital, a sum the mine could
amass in no time if shareholders forewent divs. for a short
period. As it is, the co. has £lO,OOO in hand for machinery
purposes.
* * * *
Hitherto delusive Croydon has a bright ray of
sunshine, good gold having been struck in the True Blue
at the lowest level worked on the field’s main line.
Iguana Hill prospecting co’s 600 tons returned 10750 z.,
and quite 700 oz. gold is contained in specimens just got in
the Richmond p.c.
* * * *
Opal-workers at Opalton, Q., write complaining
of the treatment they receive from opal-buyers in that
district, and want to know where they can do better.
Tire Bulletin commends this field to the attention of men
in the opal trade. The workers’ only remedy is to
encourage competition among buyers.
* * * *
Charters Towers has five mines whose average
monthly yields aggregate over 10,0000 z., but the boom
cometh not. The share-market seems almost dead. No
English co’s are being formed, and numbers of miners are
still leaving for W.A.
* * * *
Comet, Dundas (Tas.), which has yet its reputa-
tion to make, shelters another “pup.” A Melb. crowd
has turned a 160-acre stone patch into North Comet of
100,000 3s. shares. Simply built for a sympathetic flutter
when the Comet strikes its lode at 335 ft,
* * * *
The Mt. Lyell district boom waxes furious; every
mine in that region appears to be a Mt. Lyell something.
Assays showing small quantities of gold and silver and
enormous percentages of copper-are widely published.
People who are getting out remember that the winter is
near, and that where a crow can’t fly down without a
breeching is a hard place to mine in a Tas. winter.
* * * *
There is a move to amalgamate three Berry
(Vic.) mines into a big co.

Rev. Bertram Hawker, son of the lale
G. 0. Hawker, M.P., of Adelaide, is announced
to marry Miss Constance Buxton, Gov. Buxton’s
eldest unmarried daughter. It was only the other
day that the Buxtons reached Australia ; but
parsons and soldiers proverbially court hot-foot.
* * * *
“Probably for all dairy purposes in this
country the Ayrshire cow will be found to be the
best, but care should be taken to change the sire
in a herd at least once in three years,” said Gov.
Hampden at West Maitland (N.S.W.). His Ex-
cellency is clearly “on the job.” He had on
former occasions remarked that he is a large
sheep-owner, and he had also given N.S.W. his
swede-tumipy opinions. On this Maitland occa-
sion H.E. had, a few minutes before uttering the
above, highly recommended Private Enterprise,
Esq., for a big local monopoly, as against the
State. Evidently his lordship is interested in
Ayrshire cattle also. What a man ! What a
many-sided wonder! Sheep, swede - turnips,
butter, shindykits, cows, and a “ change of sire ” !
Versatility, thy name is ’Ampden !
* * * *
Sydney Daily Telegraph brevity: “Lord
Hampden thinks that the possession of capital is
needed by young Australians who would settle
on the land.” H.E. isn’t quite sure of it, you
will observe, and he only speaks of young Aus-
tralians ; he hasn’t even got so far as thinking
at old or middle-aged Australians would be the

oetter of capital under the same conditions. The
Bulletin is now waiting to hear from Gov. Hamp-
den again when he has beeome quite sure that
young Australians settling on the land need to
have capital. One of the dreadful features about
being a Gov. is that he has to make about four
speeches a week without committing himself on
any burning question, and he is even afraid to be
positive that young men settling on the land re-
quire capital, lest he thereby becomes too much of
a partisan. Almost the only thing he can say
with absolute certainty without stepping out of
his sad. and washy sphere is that there are 1760
yards in a mile.
* * * *
Gov. Buxton has so far Avoided Adelaide
theatres, and his trip to Sydney was a good excuse
for not attending two big race-meetings in Ade-
laide. Even Lieut.-Gov. Way rarely visits the
theatre, and is never seen on a racecourse. Ade-
laide society, by the way, is already showing signs
of discontent at the exclusiveness of the Govern-
ment House crowd, and the consequent prospects
of a dull winter.
* * * *
Secretary Wallington has formally parted
from Gov. Brassey, on the basis of a “ handsome
compensation. ” It wasn’t that Brassey had ceased
to love his Wally, or vice versa. Each of them
deeply regretted the “ necessity for a re-arrange-
ment of H.E.’s staff,” whereby the old secretary
is compelled to make way for a new-chum, and
soothe his sorrow with a lump sum. Hon. Ran-
dolph Capel, brother to Lady 8., is to join the
family party presently, and the capable Walling-
ton’s vacant chair will be preserved as a sacred
relic.
* * * *
There are various versions of the climax of
the revolution at Melbourne Govt. House. As
explained some months ago, before there was any
talk of Sec. Wallington leaving, it was impossible
for the pushful Earl Shaftesbury to allow anyone
to occupy a position senior to himself, and as he
began to shove and elbow, Wallington—who had
been used to having things all his own way —turned
“ shirty.”
* * * *
Captain Wallington has had unique official
experiences since his arrival in Australia. He has
been secretary to Carington, Hopetoun, Mel-
bourne Club, and Brassey, and had received the
appointment of A.D.C. to Gov. Duff before the
latter’s last illness.
* * * *
A five-figure will proved in Sydney a
couple of weeks back was that of an Irishman who
died in Ireland, but had spent years in N.S.
Wales and left considerable Australian property.
A noteworthy feature of the will was that he left
one shilling only to each of 23 members of his
family circle.

“Trilby ” is another
thorn in the cloth of the
Church— i. e., Protestant
Church. Harwood’s per-
formance of the Rev.
Thos. Bagot brings that
unctuous, worldly-min-
ded shepherd into ridi-
cule, and the audience
enjoys the spectacle.
Protestantism, anyway,
is based upon common
sense ; it recognises the
existence of mean, con-
temptible apostles of the
creed, and doesn’t scruple
to grin at their religious
absurdity. But the
R.C.’s ! No British dra-
matic author has yet
ventured to poke borak
at the shaven priest-
hood, except in plays
where the action is dated
ages back. Women re-
fuse to entertain the idea
that they pour confes-
sions, at times, into the
lecherous ear of an im-
pudent hypocrite. Their
faith teaches them to
be suspicious of all men
outside their persuasion,
and mistrustful even of
all Catholics that are
not priests. Hence the
girls are generally vir-
tuous, and the wives
mostly unhappy—apart
from the “ comforts of
religion. ”

The effect of salary-
reductions upon Melb.
tram servants is appa-
rent. Would -be cus-
tomers often signal in
vain for a car to stop,
with the result, in many
instances, that they walk
the distance they had
intended to ride, and save their threepenny-
bit. There is no doubt that the Sydney sys-
tern of fixed halting-places for the cars might
be adopted in Melbourne. It would save time
and prevent a lot of bad language,

position.” The investigators evidently thought so,

“Paddy,” J.P., to a drunk : “I’ll fine you tin
bob.” Constable, to Bench: “In default—in
default, your worship.” J.P., sternly: “What’s

The Maoriland courts are strug-
gling with a tough divorce-case. It
started in a hotel. A Northern
hotel-keeper has a pretty wife, and
among the lodgers in the house was
a gorgeous commercial-agent. The
pub.-owner came back one night after
a few days’ absence and accused his
wife of immorality with the agent,
and the lady straightwav confessed in
writing that it was alftrue. When
the case came into court the agent
called a housemaid who swore that
she slept with her mistress every
night when the old man was away,
and that none of the things alleged
m the lady’s confession ever happened.
His theory is that plaintiff and his
wife found their marriage a fa'ilure
and got up the confession by mutual
consent, and that he was selected as
the co-respondent because he was a
tall, military person of noble appear-
ance, and the lady having to allege
that she was wrongfully clasped to
somebody’s chest chose his as the
best chest available. In other words,
he was made co-respondent because
he is the kind of man that a lady of
good taste might reasonably want to
co-res pond with, and one whom she
might really enjoy swearing that she
co-responded with, even if she didn’t.
Ur, to put it more briefly, he says that
she says he is the co-respondent be-
cause she is very sorry he isn’t. The
case stands adjourned while the law
gets its second wind.

In Hobart, the other day, a little girl
stopped a lady with “ Excuse me, there’s a spider
on your dress.” The lady stood quite still while
her pocket was deftly picked. It was not for
nothing that J. Bullion exported his pickpockets
to V.D.L.
* * » *
When the New Woman does arrive at poli-
tical maturity, and when the State does create a
Grog-Selling Department, then will come the tug
of war, and the fierce party fights, as to whether
the “officers” of the Grog-Selling Department
shall be breeks-wearers or bloomeresses. In short,
the Great Barmaid Question will then convulse
the people.
* * * *
This is becoming a very common accom-
paniment to the report of the daily suicide: —
Mrs. Suicide was expected to arrive here by the
next boat from Anywhere.” It runs like a thread
of scarlet through the figured tapestry.
What has happened to a man in N.S.
Wales ! “ Fraudulently alluring from her father’s
residence, with intent to marry her, she being
under the age of 21 ” —is the wording of a charge
on which “ a lovyer so brave ” has been committed
for trial. The girl is 19 a fully developed woman
according to the laws of Nature, which are laws
of God, but an “infant,” a “ minor,”according to
the narrow laws of man. Why, in Queensland,
a girl of 18 may take up a selection, and yet
she cannot marry without her parents’ or her
guardians’ consent, or, if she has no parents or
guardians, without the permission of a magistrate
authorised to consent to the marriage of minors.
Surely a 19-year-old girl should be mistress of her
own movements ? How can she be “ lured
away”? Further, is “intent to marry” a 19-
year-old girl, even without her father’s consent, a
crime ?
The Melb. Herald linotypes have got the
«raze, too. The other day reference was made to
Trinity College as “ Trilby College.”

The Argus waxes humorous on an acci-
dent that happened to Richard Dunn, of Fitzroy,
the other day. Richard yawned so enthusiasti-
cally that he sustained lockjaw, and had to be
removed to Melb. Hospital for repairs. Richard
gets even by informing a suburban editor that he
was reading an Argus editorial when the yawn-
accident happened.

unique case proves to be The ordinary
jaw-complaint is incurable.
In the stage version of “Trilby,” author
Du Maurier has consented to be hoist with his
own petard. Readers of the book will remember
a little incidental dissertation on the absolute
purity of Art. Painters, we are told, agree in
maintaining that “nothing is so as the
nude” and so forth. In the drama laffy hurls
this fine-art doctrine at Mdme. Vinard, who asks
him to lower the studio blinds (out of considera-
tion for the girls employed at the glove-factory
opposite) whenever a model may be posing for the
‘ ‘ altogether. ” Taffy declines with scorn to con-
sider the glove-factory’s prurient blushes. A
moment later Billee rushes -in, horror-stricken, to
tell of how he has just seen Trilby sitting naked
before a roomful of students. Taffy and the
Laird are shocked likewise. Then Mdme. Vinard
points her satirical moral: “To ze pure all things
are pure —no-sing is so chaste as ze nude ! ” The
matron leaves the artists utterly routed. Their
faith in the supreme chastity of nakedness couldn’t
reconcile them to the thought that fifty young
men had just been gloating over the unclothed
figure of the woman they loved.
* * * *
A Melb. chiropodist tells a Bulletin man:
“ This Trilby business is a perfect God-send. I
have trebled my income in four months. Du
Maurier had a mission ; it is accomplished—he has
induced people to think of their feet. We chiro-
podists ought to memorialise him.”
There is no doubt that the present godless
S.A. system of education should be abolished.
How can the youths of the province become de-
cent, respectable tote-commission-agents when
they are deprived of a religious education ?

wanted.
THE SOUTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twas all on a fair summer morning,
My love pressed her lips to my mouth,
And passed without shadow of warning
To a city unfamed in the South.
When one speaks of that city, I listen,
I yearn, in love’s infinite drouth :
I watch when the wind changes southward,
I sleep with my face to the South.
C. CLYDE.
“RENDER UNTO .CAESAR.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There has been a good deal of discussion lately among
Sydney clergymen as to whether they are liable to pay
income-tax.
The parson who keeps well in view
The words of Christ on other fax,
Is apt to lose sight of the few
Texts relevant to Income-tax.
The parson dear is trying here
To save a weary world from hell;
But “ world,” you know, means all things, so
He’s bound to save his cash as well.
The church in debt, coin hard to get,
Make parsons think their people lax :
Their proper way should be to pray
For coin to pay the Income-tax.
If prayer can gain from heaven the rain,
Why should it not make coin increase ?
And Csesar Reid might then succeed
In raking in his tax in peace.
ZEF.
NO GOATS ADMITTED.
’Cos they mightn’t ‘ * act the goat, ”
Hear the hubbub and the cry
From the undergraddish throat
At.the U-ni-ver-si-ty
On Commemoration Day
(They had put it to the vote);
And they simply stayed away
’Cos they mightn’t “act the goat ” !
’Cos they mightn’t “ act the goat,”
How they raved and how they wrote !
How they tore their tickets up !
How they dregged the bitter cup !
How they sulked and stayed away ;
How they got their friends to stay ;
How they guessed the show was damned !
How the City Hall was crammed !
How each college had a fit. !
How they were not missed a bit !
How, without the undergrad,
’Twas the best success they’d had !
How the Pigfoot cut its throat
’Cos it mightn’t “ act the goat.”
DIPSO.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

The most important point for the consideration
of all persons who desire to possess photographs
of those nearest and dearest to them is with regard
to permanence rather than price. No photograph
that will turn yellow, break out in spots, or vanish
into nothingness within a few weeks can have its
value expressed in figures. When delivered, it
may shine like the enamelled mystery on a soap-
box, but the seeds of disease are there, and must
come to the surface. This may be regarded as
one of the certainties of cheap photography at per
dozen.

[?]
Sydney, April 28, 1896.
Mv Dear Moorabinda—
Sydney Polo Gymkhana last Saturday at Ken-
sington racecourse must be regarded as one of the
most successful jousts ever held by the club, for
the day was fresh, the lists full, and only one bad-
looking cropper occurred—in the costume - race,
when Dr. Scott concussed with Mr. Paterson and
looked like the “ late Dr. Scott ” for twenty
minutes, but ultimately relieved everybody’s feel-
ings by walking off the course. It was rather hard
on the audience that this serious-looking fall
should have occurred in the farcical “ costume-
race.” Nobody could help grinning at the vulgar
spectacle of Lieut. Taylor got-up as a New
Woman in black and billowy bloomers, scarlet
blouse with big sleeves, golden wig, and sailor hat
with neatly-arranged white chiffon veil, cantering
over to the disaster quite forgetful of his outrage-
ous attire. It recalled a scene I once saw behind
the curtain at a pantomime, when a fainting fairy
was being brought round by two living vegetables
and the hind legs of an elephant.
Next to the costume-race for comicality came
the tandem heats. Some of the chop-tailed little
gee-gees in the lead—anyone who chops a horse’s
tail in Australia should get six months’ hard and
then be transported to Hingland for life—scarcely
seemed to know what was wanted, and conse-
quently the shaft horse forged level with and even
ahead of his leader more often than not. I was
glad to see that one or two polo youths had
humanely left a switch on their mounts, regardless
of its anti-English appearance. The needle-thread-
ing race went off quicker than usual, suggesting
that some of the damsels at the barrier had
supplied themselves with spring needles for the
occasion. By the way, how very domestic it
looks to see the married amateur jockey receiving
the needle from his wedded wife ! One almost
expects to see her utilising the thread to sew a
button on for him when ne returns to scratch.
The musical ride, by members of the Defence
Force, was a pretty spectacle cleverly manoeuvred,
and just circusy enough to be characteristic of
Australian “sojerdom.”
There was an excellent lawn attendance. His
Ex. turned up nursing an umbrella with a golf-
stick handle, and looking much brighter and more
sunburnt than usual. Gazing on fat pigs and
prize pumpkins in various country towns—as Lord
H. has been and is still doing—appears to agree
with the V.R. constitution in all its bearings. I
think, too, that His Ex. ’s genius for mingling with
the silk-petticoated throng and conversing with
the society female in her best hat is likely to keep
his memory green when Jamberoo is the Federal
city. Lady H. wore a grey dress with a white
vest d la seagull, the pink roses in her bonnet
being hardly on kissing terms with her red
famp, and I was also surprised to see that Her
lx. humped her own brown wrap. The private
secretary who wrote about the attributes of Aus-
tralian Governors might now favor the world with
a disquisition on what “suites” are hired for if
not to relieve Madame of all responsibility regard-
ing wraps ? I never saw Lady Jersey carrying
her own waterproof. Miss Brand wore black
crepon on pottery-blue ; with white bib collar and
cuffs and black and white hat.
Society was exhaustively represented on the
lawn. The pretty wife of an Australian Croesus
wore a black coat and skirt with purple silk vest
beaded gorgeously, and a purple toque with black
suggestions, and chaperoned a Sydney belle not
long back from Europe, with apparently some
wild-goose’s feathers stuck in her hat. Sydney’s
best-dressed society woman almost escaped notice
in a sealskin sacque finished off with fur, and
somebody else in pale-heliotrope had nose and lips
en suite, for the wind was high, cruelly revealing
sometimes that society had not pulled up its socks.
There was a great diversity of male attire, from
the white riding-breeches suggestive of flour-bags
to knickerbockers ; one plump youth, by the way,
showed great thrift by the masterly manner m
which he held a half-smoked cigar behind him
what time he shook hands with Lady H. Sydney
cigar-stump hunters must be experiencing evil
times when a man prefers clinging to three inches
of sixpenny smoke to raising his hat to a lady.
The recent contretemps at Perth, W.A., when
Governor Smith omitted to quench the Great Aus-
tralian Thirst of the local soldiers lined upi at his
daughter’s wedding, and has been apologising, per
aide-de-camp, to every big-wig in the barracks
almost ever since—reminds me that when Gov.
Brassey “invited” the Sydney nurses aboard the
Sunbeam the ministering angels were charged six-
pence a head admission, and did not even get a
bun out of it at that. Nor would that have
mattered a row of pins if there had been no re-
freshments at all—only some of the sisters Dora,
peeping through a skylight, saw a society company
revelling in tea and tarts in the saloon while they
starved on deck. Vice-regality should never for-
get that modem Australian “ loyalty ” is largely
a question of eating and drinking. But, apropos
nurses, have you ever remarked that Florence
Nightingale, in her square-shouldered black cloak
narrowing in to the feet, is gruesomely suggestive
of the shape of a coffin ? And I saw one the
other day with a bunch of tuberoses in the front
of her frock—just like the little bouquet the truest
friend of a dead woman sorrowfully places on the
name-plate to conceal the age of the deceased.
# Her many admirers and well-wishers in Austra-
lia will be glad to hear (I have it from London)
that the erstwhile somewhat greaseful Ada Cross-
ley, who promised to be beef to the heels in a few
years’ time (like pretty nearly every other typical
contralto), is fining down in the big smoke, and is
not half so florid of face and generous of outline
as of yore—besides being much slighter in the
figure. At the Lemmone concert, at the Queen’s
Hall, she was very elegantly gotten up in an in-
describable but very smart black-and-gold gown,
which has earned her the expensive reputation of
being the best-dressed woman on the English
concert-platform.
Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall was, last Wednesday,
the happy hunting-ground of a band of Society
amateurs, who lifted up their voices in aid of the
Home for Working Gentlewomen, before a fashion-
able house headed by Her Ex. in wonga-pigeon
blue brocade, with darker velvet sleeves and cream
chiffon vest, and her hair arranged like an oyster-
pate. Lady H. was in ambush behind a huge
bouquet of multi-colored roses, in which gracious
shelter she yawned almost unperceived, while
Bishop Smith, out in the open, had to try and
make his forty winks look like silent prayer. The
platform was artistically draped in rainbow stuff,
with palms and pampas-grass and huge baskets of
’mums. Perhaps the most soothing thing about
the show was that no flowers found their way up
to the women. The spectacle of a squally she-
vocalist being deluged with herbs by her friends
and near relations only looks as if they were
accentuating their gratitude to her for leaving off.
The chiefest vocalist on the programme was Dr.
Maguire, whose well-cultivated operatic baritone
made the success of the evening with “Eri Tu,”
which gained a big encore, and a cavatina from
“La Traviata ” —Mrs. Maguire, in black satin
and green jet embroidery, contributing an excel-
lent accompaniment. Otherwise the programme
was principally the Hon. Francis Egerton, A.D.C.,
and Mr. D. Hopkins, R.N., private see. to the
Admiral—judging by the applause they got. Capt.
Egerton (fiercely accompanied by Miss Ida Gilles)
sang a love-ditty in a most lady-like manner with
one toe poised like a dancing-master, but, of
course, he was wildly encored, and turned up again
and won the respect of the audience for contri-
buting an encore one verse long—or, I suppose I
ought to say, short—until it leaked out that his
accompanist had to out him off because she
couldn’t play any more of it at sight. The naval
youth picked out his own accompaniments. His
song was comic and sung in a tone that appeared
to be the still small voice of conscience. His
classics were “The tin gee-gee;” a ditty about
the probability of the society girl taking out her
teeth and hanging her golden hair on the gas on
retiring (enthusiastically received) and “ Our
Parents,” which was the cleverest of the batch.
Mr. Hopkins appeared to be a huge success and
turned his end of the Y.M.C.A. into a music-hall.
A respectable old gentleman was heard calling out
(but ineffectually) for more comic songs whenever
the loo’tenant showed signs of leaving off his
Lonnenesque lay. One touch of the Tivoli makes
the whole world kin. A Liszt solo was amusing
because of the labial and other gyrations of the
lady-performer—who made music as a baker
makes dough. Nathless she was applauded.
“ Persons and not performances ” was the general
rule for applause on this occasion.
The wedding of Miss Ethel Turner, the popular
young Australian authoress, and Mr. H. R. Cur-
lewis, a rising Sydney barrister, took place at St.
John’s Church, Gordon, on 22nd inst., in the pre-
sence of a huge wedding-party that completely
swamped the little church, where the decorations
were of white chrysanthemums to match the
bride’s cheeks, becomingly pale for the cere-
mony. The pretty blonde bride wore white
satin and chiffon, and carried a fragrant posy,
and her four maids were alike in cream with
chiffon fixings and straw hats en suite, and carry-
ing bouquets of white and yellow chrysanthemums
and ferns. After the ceremony a reception was
held at Bukyangi, and afternoon tea was served
in a marquee on the lawn, while the handsome
wedding presents were displayed in the dining-
room. After a lively interval of congratulations,
unharassed by set speeches of any kind, Mr. and
Mrs. Curlewis left for their honeymoon, the clever
little bride going away in a grey gown with shot
Eurple ribbons, hat en suite. Mosman's Bay will
e the serious home of the happy, young-looking
pair.
At the gaslight wedding last week of a popular
Sydney medico —who married a nurse, of course, as
nearly all the medicine-men do, propinquity being
one of the chief factors in love—the very pretty
bride almost fainted before the ceremony, and no
wonder. The church was a seething mass of
sightseers who occupied every coign of vantage,
stood on the seats, and blocked the aisles, and it
took all the steering in the world to get the lady
to the altar with her veil in one piece. So un-
seemly was the crush that the minister sharply
lectured the mob on the way they were crowding
the church. I really think weddings should he
held in camera. A mob of strange
and raping round a girl most y 8 P

am one with Max Nordau on the .mpropnet^ol
modern marriage rites. And yet, I Ipo >
a police-court point of view, it woul would
marriages contracted altogether sub «wo-it would
give the amorous bigamist too many chances.
Apropos weddings. A well-known Sydneyite
lately went up-country to take unto 1 _
wife, and as everybody in the bush township knew
what he had come for, he was subjected to much
chaff. The day before the wedding he was para-
ding the main street with his best man, when he
all at once became aware that Blankeyville was
convulsed with merriment as one unit. Booking
behind them the spruce pair saw that “e “aker
man was following them up with the gotten g
white wedding-cake on a tray upon his head.
Then that procession became the most depressed
one ever seen since the last man was driven to the
gallows sitting on his own coffin.
Talking of the newly-married—a pair of honey-
mooners were travelling from Sydney by tram
the other day, and, at one of the stations, the
bridegroom left the bride alone for a little while.
Imagine the bride’s flattered feelings (it is every
bride’s ambition to look as little newly-wed as
possible) when up popped the guard and asked
her if she would mind travelling in the ladies
compartment, as he wished to reserve a carriage
for a newly-married couple ? But the lady rose
to the occasion, and diplomatically replied that
she was travelling with a gentleman and could not
very well take him into a ladies’ carriage ! But,
mind you, that hoodwinking reply was only
possible with a bride conscious of having no rice in
her hat-brim.
A suburban female ‘ ‘ literary ’ club held a dis-
cussion the other day as to when was the best age
to marry ! All the young members, thought the
bride should be eighteen and the groom twenty-
two, while the members with long horse-teeth, of
the English sort, sacrificed the lady at thirty (!)
and the man at thirty-six. But I think neither
party hit the mark—one went over it, and the
other below it, to my mind. Boy-and-girl mar-
riages have more drawbacks than admirers
of youth imagine and possibly the most
solemn of all is that, when the juvenile husband
finds it necessary, in the ordinary course of events,
to fly for the doctor at 2 a.in., he is likely to be
received with suspicion by that potentate, who
takes the distracted youth for a bad boy trying to
“pull his leg,” as they used to say in the Dean
case. I heard the other day of a boy bridegroom
who refused to go for medical assistance, alleging
that it would not avail, as the medico would be
almost certain not to take him seriously ! So a
married he-relative, who looked the part, was
sent flying in his carpet-slippers instead.
Apropos doctors—a lady who recently under-
went an operation in a Sydney private hospital
tells the story of her visions after an opium-injec-
tion, and thereby clearly reveals why women-
doctors haven’t Buckley’s chance with their own
sex outside of a Zenana. “ When I came to,” she
says, “ the doctor was stooping over me with an
anxious expression. ‘No more opium for you,
little woman, ’he said. ” There you have a great
trunk of medicine-man philosophy. Doctresses
will never have their own way with their own sex;
they cannot say “ Now, little woman ” the least
bit effectively.
Australian relic-hunters, whose mania it is to
secure a button off Captain Cook’s waistcoat or a
footmark of La Perouse, will be sorry to hear that
so sterling a curio as the first watch made out of
Australian gold (to the order of the gold-discoverer,
Hargreaves) is not in the Museum, where it ought
to be, but is casually worn by an irreverent youth
in the bush, who would just as soon have a Water-
bury to apprise him that dinner was ready. Aus-
tralians are curiously uninterested in the early
relics of their native land—perhaps because so
many of ’em (I mean the relics) look lonely with-
out a gaol in the middle-distance. Which, some-
how, reminds me that a new collecting mania has
arisen in Sydney—for the tops of matchboxes.
Preparations are afloat in Sydney for the hold-
ing of a charity ball in aid of the Jewish Education
Board, which is suffering from overdraft and wants
■£loo to enable it to go to sleep o’ nights. The
ball, which is in the hands of a competent Jewish
committee, certainly ought to be a great success.
But what is one ball to an Israelite ?
The annual ball in aid of the working and fac-
tory gfirls club will come off, under vice-regal,
naval, and civil patronage and presence, at Pad-
dington Town Hall on Monday, 4th May, at 9
o’clock. The factory girls’ club ball is always an
intensely toney fixture, and tickets for it are stuck
in the mirrors of the truly fashionable every year.
Mrs. (Lieut.) Harvey and Mrs. Villeneuve Smith
are hon. secs, to what promises to be the usual
brilliant turn-out.
Apropos of balls, it is evidently “the high
society plan ” (as they say in “ Club Life ”) that
when hubby is seriously ill madame should show
her wifely anxiety for his welfare by doing more
dancmg than she ever did before at all the big
public balls. But let us be charitable. This is an
age of Indian religions in fashionable circles.
Perhaps such ladies belong to the order of the
dancing Dervishes who turn themselves into tee-
totums to mollify the Fates ?

“ Longstaff’s life-size portrait of Melb.’s late
Mayor Snowden is another win for the Australian
painter of ‘ The Sirens.’ By his splendid painting
of the robes—the mere sight of the rich brown fur
would be comforting on a cold day, while the sym-
bolic gold chain would make Bill Sikes’s fingers
itch—and the fine force of an atmospheric back-
ground, he has lent ‘ his Worship ’ a dignity which
the casual passer-by can’t easily detect. The
Council sets so awesome a value on its gold chain
that it was sent up by the caretaker with special
instructions not to let it out of his possession.
Longstaff dressed up the caretaker in the gold
chain, and saved himself from a fit of melancholy
by thus painting the subject by proxy, for poor
Snowden took his presentation portrait so seri-
ously that he grew woe-begone in his efforts to
look the right thing.
“ Of the ten ardent young Melbourne Anglicans
who, 13 years or so ago, took solemn vows of
eternal celibacy, tall Ernest Selwyn Hughes, now
chief curate of St. Peter’s, is the only one up to
date who hasn’t retrograded. The devotional
Spicer, of cross-bespangled All Saints, East St.
Kilda, led this crusade against the wiles ‘of the
world, the flesh, and the devil,’ and the first to
secede was the lately-elected Bishop of Brisbane,
then popularly known as ‘Jack Stretch.’ He had
been engaged to a young lady in Geelong before
the sacerdotal fever seized him; and when he
begged the damsel to consider herself supplanted
by a more abstract kind of bride,'she imme-
diately fell ill, and the impressionable Stretch,
finding that he would have to break either
a heart or a vow, chose the latter sin.
At intervals followed another and another
defaulter. Then Spicer himself fell a victim to
feminine bewitchments, and this brought about
general disorganisation. Now it is reported that
Rev. Evelyn Snodgrass, Lady Clarke’s brother, is
engaged, ■ after all along proclaiming ‘never,
never !’ in protestation. Old Canon Gregory, of
All Saints’, had a lively time with these would-be
monks while the craze lasted—their ecclesiastical
pranks nearly put him in his grave. They all but
celebrated Mass, openly prayed to the Virgin, and
were great upon vestments and processions. Now
these things remain as humorous memories chiefly
to the girls who onetime sighed over the per-
formers.
“ Edith Crane, the Princess Trilby, owes much
of the man-worship accorded her to the fact that
she wears no stays in either the first or third acts.
The spectacle of one real, live, palpitating woman,
especially if decollete, will bring the average man
to a state of subjection in a quicker time than all
the weaknesses of Solomon encased in stiff whale-
bone.
“ Lady Brassey has grown noticeably older since
she has been in Melbourne. She shows grey by
the side of her fringe. Yet she is only 38.
“ Dr. Freda Gamble, one of the newly-appointed
women resident surgeons at Melb. Hospital, is one
of the tallest girls in Melbourne, and one of the
strongest. She hasambition enough to accomplish
anything, and the tireless industry of a traction-
engine.
“ By the way, House-Surgeon Molloy, of Melb.
Hospital, is too wary to express any opinion about
the admission of the two lady medicos who will b a
under his charge. ‘ Bless their little hearts,’ is hi
stock comment. Now, there are several ways of
blessing people’s hearts.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
A Corowa (N.S.W.) medico insists on all
his children being “ Trilbies ” in fact and feet.
The other day Melb. saw his little daughter pat-
tering barefooted down the Block with him in
pouring rain. -
The Eight Hours Day “banquet” at
Melb. Exhibition building was commendable for
its native simplicity and economical principles.
Victorian fruits, cakes, wines and lager beer were
the refreshment provided, and the guests didn’t
number more than 175, all told.
Prof. Harry Brookes Allen, who was at
the bottom of the whole stramash about women as
residents of a general hospital like the Melb.
Hospital, is the only graduate of Melb. University
who was first in the first class with honors through-
out his course.
Solicitor Sam Lyons, Premier Turner’s
partner, trips to Europe, thus doubling Turner's
work. Now, as Turner is already suffering from
over-work, Lyons’ present trip probably means
turners early retirement from office, and this
rneans either Isaacs or Peacock in the Premier s
boots.

has come down on its ear with a thud. Forty

the lory and Ass persuasion. Also, six other

o 19 Ministerialists, 12 Labor members and 15
lory Oppositionists. Batchelor and Premier

Premier Kingston was nominated for
West Adelaide by his wife. This presumably as
an antidote to the secret-stabbing tactics of the
National Ass.
Melbourne rumor has it that Speight’s
friends intend to engineer him into the billet of
Minister of Railways via Parliament. But it will
scarcely come off.
According to Siam Free Press, Vic.
Govt, has been trying, per its bagman, Jerome
Dyer, to trade off its two dubious gunboats to the
Siamese Govt, for .£30,000.
“Are you in favor of having only moral
men in Parliament?” was asked of a candidate
for S.A. Assembly. “ I am,” said the candidate,
if we can raise a quorum ! ”
“ Premier Forrest and I were friends for
20 years, yet he dismissed me in my night-shirt.”
—Ex-Works Commissioner Venn. Westralia is
behind in everything, it seems—even pyjamas.
Five out of the eight Q. Cabinet Ministers
are lawyers. A new epithet for Leperland,
Kanakaland, Mongrelia, &c. Sharkland. And
the Nelson Government is the Shark Ministry.
Sydney Daily Telegraph : “It is esti-
mated that 1000 rebels have been killed in the
various actions in Matabeleland, and that 300
white settlers have been murdered.” Bulletin
italics.
Victorian : “ Our Leg. Council just follows
the programme laid down by Graham Berry, when
he demanded a Nominee House because a Repre-
sentative one would claim equality with the
People’s Chamber.”
Eight-Hours day isn’t a bank holiday in
Victoria. It doesn’t matter much, perhaps, but
the proclamation might induce an odd bank to
close, which would practically mean a holiday for
hundreds of shop-assistants.
German steam tonnage from Hamburg to
Australia has risen from 24,162 tons (7 vessels) in
1886 to 111,691 (26 vessels) in ’95. Likewise sailing
tonnage, irrespective of coke-ships for Port Pirie,
has gained 9812 tons in the ten years.
Some of the Boodlewraithian election
dodges were most pitiable. In one case a quarter
of a democratic Northern constituency containing
several hundred votes was not allowed a polling-
booth, and electors had to tramp in, two and three
miles, to the centre of the city. On the other
hand, a “ safe ” country constituency was allowed
a polling-booth in a bush township, where a sum
total of one vote was recorded. In the North,
men rode 40, 50 and 79 miles to record their votes,
and in one Central constituency 40 men rode
90 miles to vote for the Labor candidate.

One of the chief features of the S.A. elec-
tions, last Saturday, was that the working-woman
voter almost always asked the poll-clerk : Are
you quite sure that nobody will be able to find out
how I vote ”? The fear of “the missus J led to
caution. Also, Premier Kingston says that the
provision for postal-voting by female voters who
reside more than three miles from a polling-place
is liable to be grossly abused, because servants
may be coerced by their mistresses.

The recent J.P. nominations of the Reid
Govt, contain a far larger percentage of country
journalistic appointees to the magistracy than any
similar list ever promulgated in Australia. Thus
does the God-fearing Ministry make friends with
the printers’ devil!
The new President of the Orange Free
State exhorts every Afrikander to stick tight to
his Bible and his Gun.” This confiscation of
British sentiment by the arrogant Boers is an
insult to the whole Anglo-Saxon race. It must
have been inspired by the Kaiser, whose grand-
father was a whale for piety-cum-slaughter.

Conservatives were in power, the province lost
14,000 people by emigration; from 1891 to 1895, under
a Democratic Government, it gained over 15,000
by immigration. Yet the Conservatives of that
past Ministry—those that remain of them—are
