The whirligig of politics. The M‘Millan
trumpets himself as a Republican ! Or else what
means this (S.M. Herald) :
Mr. M‘Millan : . . . The time is coming when Aus-
tralia will march from the mere seat of a colonial life to
the seat of a nation. We cannot be a nation now-a-days
without taking upon ourselves the responsibilities of a
nation. And, in this great Southern Sea, if Australia is
to be a nation, it must be one among the many nations of
the world.
Another N.S.W. solicitor was committed
for trial last week on a charge of unlawfully
prigging the trust money. The law is getting into
a beastly habit of sending the alleged fraudulent
solicitor for trial just like any other man. It has
been a long time getting into the habit, but it
really seems to be arriving there pretty satisfac-
torily at last, and as a result there has been quite
a rush of Gentlemen One to gaol within the last
month or so.
Charles Beer, Kingston’s opponent at West
Adelaide, the wild-haired person who wanted to
float a loan of £1,000,000,000, didn’t go to the
poll in any serious fashion. A few days
before the election the poor fellow seems to
have gone off the small remains of his head, and
he was arrested as a pauper lunatic and sent to an
asylum, where he is now floating his enormous
loan in peace and quietude. He got 32 votes all
the same.
The Germans are going in for a serious
attempt to land Westphalian coal in England at
prices that will compete with the British article,
and with reduced freights and practically nominal
rail charges they reckon it can be done. The
German’s desire to cut out the Englander in com-
merce and manufactures has become a passion,
and some day or other it will probably be decided
that the world isn’t large enough to hold these two
States. Then something will break.
Nearly everything that hard, unmitigated
lying and slander can do to overset the M.L.
democratic Ministry is being done by the National
Ass in the present campaign. One fine specimen
statement is as follows :—“ The Seddon Govern-
ment raise money on Treasury bills in the colony
at 4 and per cent., and when they have reached
the limit permitted by law these bills are ‘con-
verted ’ in London into permanent debt. ” To
which Premier Seddon replies that since the
democratic Ministry came into power the issue of
Treasury bills has never reached the limit fixed by
law, and none of the Treasury bills have been
converted into permanent debt. It was the old
National Ass Government which did these things,
and that animal now blandly charges its own acts
to its opponents and says reproachfully that these
doings are very wrong indeed.
The Dean of Melbourne on foreign mis
sions :
He was pleased to see that the Jelap Pass had been
forced, and the Cross carried even into Thibet.
He also impressed upon people the necessity of
pushing forward with the work in the Soudan,
and with the evangelisation of Uganda. And in
Matabeleland surely, where the red-coated mis-
sionaries are carrying the “maxims” of Chris-
tianity to the “pore heathen.” The “cross”
follows the sword, and trade follows the cross.
Every missioner is a commissioner, and of such is
the Kingdom.
The Q. elections have again em-
phasised the fact that the miners and
shearers are the backbone of democracy.
Two-thirds of the Labor representation
comes from these quarters. Governmental
strongholds are the coast towns, dependent
largely on political patronage.
Colonising Kaiser Wilhelm has an eye on
German New Guinea, as well as on the Transvaal.
Prospector Braddon, looking for gold on the Mem-
pare River, was met by the Governor of the Ger-
man colony, who “informed us that he had been
advised from Berlin that a party of prospectors
had left Cairns, and he was instructed by the
Emperor to see that they did not encroach on
German territory, and take a lot of gold away
with them.” Germany must have some sharp
spies in Australia.
W Gaunson —David’s little brother Bill—
the other night, endeavored to induce the metro-
politan committee of the A.N.A., Melb., to call
upon Purves, Q.C., to assert his loyalty to the
Throne and Person, and take an oath of allegiance
to his Gracious Queen and Imperialism generally,
or words to that effect, or be hounded out of the
A.N.A., and repudiated with a cold shudder. The
attempt failed, but the fact that it was made, and
the fact that all over the country leading lights
of the Natives’ Ass have struggled to the
surface to repudiate Purves’s prediction of an
Australian republic 2000 years hence, shows that
the A.N.A. has become a mere machine for tuft-
hunters and a thing to provoke the contempt of
the native with a spine of his own.
Foxton, who takes Barlow’s place as Q.
Minister for Lands, is an obscure country lawyer,
with a political reputation for cunning, and a
fine record of solid votes for Boodlewraitii. Also,
he is supposed to have driven bullock - drays
through most of the weak places in the couple of
score Land Acts which the province enjoys, and
to be gifted with some kind of craving to codify
that vast mass of legislation. For the rest, he is
simply a reliable mediocrity.
Egypt is starting a State Loan Depart-
ment, after the Maoriland model, to supply loans
to agriculturists at 6 per cent., the private money-
lender’s rate having hitherto been from 36 to 70
per cent. The Indian Govt, is alleged to be
incubating a similar idea. Evidently it is getting
nearly too late to rave against the cheap money
scheme as a rash and socialistic experiment only
advocated by revolutionists and irresponsible
people who sleep in Sydney Domain.
•The glory of Melb. Trades Hall is indeed
departed. Up to last year the programme of the
Eight-Hours procession was always printed in full,
and the coming event got at least a column of
notice. This time a four-inch paragraph in the
Age, mentioning that the annual show would
follow a certain route, was all the preliminary puff
it received from the dailies. The Argus scorned
to remember that a labor festival was at hand,
merely remarking the fact of a public holiday
having been gazetted.
Sargood, as representative man of the
tyrannous Vic. Leg. Council, retires in Aug., and
may be defeated by a vigorous democratic canvass.
Barbour, who ran Murray Smith close at the As-
sembly elections for Hawthorn, is already an-
nounced as a probable candidate for S. Yarra,
Sargood’s province. It was in this province that
Godfrey defeated Boomster Davies last year on
the democratic ticket. Whatever happens, Sargood
will have a big fight this year. He is a clever
man, and a strong man, but his strength and
cleverness are pitted against the democracy.
A number of Yic. military officers, thirst-
ing for glory, are agitating Govt, to pay for the
transport of a local regiment to South Africa,
where, it is understood, the Empire is being
nigger-assailed. The heroes would go “on their
own, ” but they wish the matter to be an affair of
State. It is declared that the despatch of a Vio-
torian force would “ convince the world of the
unity of the Empire,” and have a soothing effect
generally on the European situation. The envy
inspired in Victoria by Sydney’s Soudan goat and
jackass is responsible for all.
In last issue appeared announcements from
two private speculators who propose applying to
Parliament for authority to build the long-expected
North Shore (Sydney) bridge. Also, there are
rumors that a tunnel syndicate is again in the
field. This violent rush of syndicates is evidence
that there is likely to be much good money in the
enterprise, and the fact that they desire the conces-
sion so badly is a solid reason why they shouldn’t get
it, and why the State should take up the matter
itself without further delay. The new syndicate,
like all previous ones, wants to annex the one good
crossing-place (from Dawes to Miller’s Point),
which means that sooner or later the State will
have to buy out its monopoly at an exorbitant
price to make its own railway connection, there-
fore the State might as well do the work at the
beginning. The amount required, estimated at
<£500,000, could be borrowed in Sydney at a low
rate on very short notice, and as the present syndi-
cate would borrow its money in London the
country would do better to owe it locally than any
other way. No great work is ever done in Aus-
tralia without owing the money somewhere, so the
lo m element may be taken for granted.

People who want to have a splendid night’s
fun at lowest possible cost on Queen’s birth-night,
should either call on, or at once write to, that
well-known old firm, Myers & Solomon, Market-
street (two doors from York-st.), Sydney, whose
fireworks have been illuminating the Australian
skies for the past 40 years. A magnificent cargo
of the newest fireworks is just to hand, and such
old favorites as the festive Chinese gun, the
dazzling Catherine wheel, and the many species
of soaring rockets which paint the startled firma-
ment with more than rainbow colors amid a galaxy
of gorgeous stars. Full particulars on page 4 this
issue.*

Personal Items.
London journalism lately descended to
“An Interview with Mr. Cecil Rhodes’s Old
Nurse.”
James Service was once a schoolmaster.
One of his pupils was Capt. Currie, cutest of
Melb. shipowners, who sat on the Lormer Board.
The Tory Yic. M.L.A., Downward, has
taken Punch M’Kinley’s place as silent-member
of Vic. Parliament. Silence is a Downward family
attribute.
Attorney-General Want, who has written
and published a lot of good “ copy ” on Japanese
questions, is again burning midnight oil in pursuit
of literary fame.
The widow of the late N.S.W. Chief-
Justice Martin is now residing with her family at
Clarens, Switzerland. Clarens was the name
selected by the late Chief-Justice for his Potts
Point house, where he died.
Vic. Railways Commissioner MATHIESON.
Photo, by Poulsen.
G. D. Clark, ex-M.P. and G.W.X. of the
Cold-Water Bashi Bazouks of N.S.W. : “ But
I’ve been a teetotaller all my life, sir.” Premier
Reid : “ Try something else, old man.”
Melb. demise : Nehemiah Wimble, 40
years in the Lands Department, ending as Secre-
tary. For as long he demonstrated the superfluity
of paid clergy, as a Wesleyan preacher.
Miss Annie Catto, daughter of the late
John Catto, a well-known rich Victorian, has just
married Major St. Clair Pemberton, R.E., arising
soldier. Another link in the “golden chain which
binds,” &c., &e.
In a vice-regal way of speaking, there is
said to be only one closer-fisted person (considering
his strong provocation to be open-handed) than
the Gov. of —no matter —somewhere up south.
His wife is the other person.
Henniker Heaton, it is rumored, means to
put himself at the head of a Divorce-reform party
in the Commons. The idea is to extend the
grounds for divorce, and simplify the legal pro-
cedure on the lines of Shiels’s Act.
At latest, Julian Salomons, Q.C., was still
at the Hotel Bristol, London, giving charming
little dinners to charming ladies and gentlemen.
He was still great on anecdotes, none of which
would bring a blush to a maiden’s cheek.
The Cooktown Independent has only
qualified approval for Gov. Lamington : “ He is
not a practised speaker, but there is the same ring
of sincerity in his words as in those uttered by a
Presbyterian parson on the eve of Communion.”
Vic. Chief Justice thinks cycling undigni-
fied for a man whose moustache is the pillar of
Justice. To work off flesh, he has taken to row-
ing. His new boat, to carry two 14st. fats, is
built in two parts, division amidships, to make it
easily portable.
“Joe” Ivess, the M.L. “ rag-planter, ”
has hatched another paper—the sixth in two
years. The great majority of papers in Maori-
land appear to have been founded by Ivess at
one time or another. Mr. Ivess plants and sells
on cabbage-growing principles.
Miss Collins, murdered at Manly the other
day by her jealous lover, saw the body of Mrs.
Down, murdered by her jealous husband, and
said, “ How terrible to look like that!” Two days
afterwards she was herself occupying the slab next
to Mrs. Down’s in the Morgue.
Despite titles, people do really honor solid
worth before decorated shoddy. So far, only LlOO
has been raised for a monument to the late J. B.
Patterson, K.C.M.G., whereas over L6OO was
subscribed to enable Labor-leader Trenwith to
have his eyes treated in England.
C.E. Bishop Green (Grafton and Armidale,
N.S.W.) desires his clergy uniformly to adopt
“the Eastern position.” Sun-worshippers, evi-
dently. The next move should be the ‘ ‘ reverence, ”
or kowtow, whenever passing in front of the altar.
It’s good for sciatica and the knee-pan.
Democrat Gresley Lukin’s permanent ab-
sorption by Tory Wellington Post suspends
for some time the dream of establishing a
full-voiced Radical Brisbane daily. Buzacott,
who followed Lukin into the Courier, and
is now out in the fresh air, intermittently
talks of starting something, but the leopard can’t
change his spots, and two Couriers in the Queens-
land capital would be like two Sundays falling at
the same time.
Mount Morganaire Skarratt, who has ceased
to dye, and is now a nice, fatherly-looking old
gentleman, has changed his mind about returning
to Sydney, and has taken a large house in Queen’s
Gate, London, which is fast becoming a kind of
Botany Bay, so many Australians foregather
there.
The conversation at the Library Conference
in Melbourne was throughout couched in the
worst possible English. Overheard : “We ain’t
got no such system of cataloguing as yourn, but
then we ain’t got no need for it, ’avin’ a smaller
library. ”
Sir Claude Champion de Crespigny has
just published memoirs of his sporting career.
He appears to be an amateur boxer, swimmer,
balloonist, and steeplechase rider of minor distinc-
tion, and his name has been connected with the
fatal noose. He was an amateur assistant hang-
man some years ago.
The correct pronunciation of the names of
some of the Australian Governors is a matter de-
serving more consideration than it receives.
Hampden is Hamden—without a “p”; Brassey
is Brass-eye ; Gerard-Smith is G —rard ; Fowe!) -
Buxton (but this everybody knows) is Fool-Buxton,
the “ w ” not being sounded.
Percy Spence and wife return to Australia
per incoming mail. Spence’s London friends much
regret his decision to return to Australia, for he
had already made a name for himself at the “ big
smoke,” and had plenty of work, but both he and
his wife found the English winter trying, and the
move to sunnier skies was imperative.
A big effort is being made to drum up a
decent testimonial in Melbourne for Lormer, J.P.,
who worked the crusade against his rocky brethren
of the Bench. It is a good cause, and the fact
that Lormer is called upon to fight a libel-action,
as the result of his public-spirited attitude, gives
him an additional claim upon the lovers of clean
justice.
Old Jack Monahan, Melbourne’s illiterate
millionaire, once heard a guest at a London d inner
say that the champagne was “ simply splendid !
nectar for the gods ! ” The observant Monahan
noted the remark as useful for a future occasion,
and, later on, being asked what he thought of the
wine, replied “ Grand, sor. Grand! Simply
splendid ! Be me sowl, it’s a necktie for the
Almighty ! ”
A Broken Hill paper published a full re'
port of the banquet tendered by the citizens to
Governor Carington, in the days when fortunes
were being made out of silver-shares. The proof-
reader was so knocked-up with the hard night’s
work that when he came to the end of the banquet-
report proofs he wrote the words “Thank God ! ”
The comp., when correcting the galley-proof, made
it read “ Lord Carington then left for Singleton,
thank God ! ” The comp, didn’t suffer this time,
but the proof-reader was “ sacked.”
There has just left
Queensland the last of
a somewhat notable
family—the Archers of
Gracemere. The Ar-
chers were of Scottish
descent, though the
family settled in Nor-
way 70 years ago.
There were nine bro-
thers (three now dead),
who all at various times
resided in what is now
Queensland (some be-
fore its separation from
N.S. Wales). Seven of
them, at one time or
another, managed
Gracemere station,
which was taken up by
Charles and William
Archer in ’53, and
stocked in ’55. Brother
Alexander perished
with his wife at the
Quetta wreck. Brother
Colin, now in Norway,
is a naval architect, and
designed the Fram, in
which Nansen started
for the North Pole.
Then came Thomas,
the late Agent-General,
and Archibald, mem-
ber for Rockhampton
in the last Q. Parlia-
ment and sometime
Colonial Treasurer. Ar-
chibald first put up for
Rockhampton in ’67,
and was defeated by T.
Fitzgerald, a well-
known Northern poli-
tician. Fitzgerald
elected to sit for Mac-
kay, which also chose
him, and Archer sue
ceeded to the Rock
hampton seat a few
weeks later. Out of the
Gracemere estate the
city of Rockhampton
was carved, but the
squatter Archers main-
tained their popularity in the new city for
many years. Archibald’s ratting on the Separa-
tion question sealed his political career in the
North. He leaves Bananaland, at the ripe age of
76, on a visit to Norway.
At poor old Parkes’s recent sale was
sold his copy of Stepniak’s “ Story of a Nihilist,”
which he had bought in London in Oct., 1888.
The old man was then hobnobbing with royalty
and the aristocracies almost hourly; but he had
been so impressed by the tragic story of Sophia
Perovskaia that he then and there pencilled on the
end pages the first draft of his “ Beauteous
Terrorist.” Apparently, even in Parkes, there
was illustrated Emerson’s saying that a man’s
youth never dies. On the very day that he pen-
cilled those lines he was the guest of B ntlsh
Premier, and dined with the Prince o •
The humorless Snowden regards himself
with amazement. It is almost incredible thing
Snowden, that within the space of Jour years
can have been three times Mayor of A ’ f
addition to becoming a knight and a “ember o
Yic. Leg. Council. It does seem incredible. Even
the knighting of Bingy Benjy, after he had been
three successive mayors, was not much m
derful than Snowden’s case.
The King Charles’s head of Gov. Brassey’s
speeches is that well-contented potentate’s decease
pa. A rough estimate shows the old gentleman in
seven out of ten of the gubernatorial efforts. •
Hampden only once or twice smote the family
sepulchre, and Gov. Buxton lets the dead lie,
afraid probably of creating a smell of beer,
Brassey, when talking to a crowd, appears to keep
one eye fixed constantly on his dad.
Purves, Q. 0., dealt mercifully with old
Jock Nimmo in his remarks (Mirams v. Argus)
upon the management of Melbourne Premier Per-
manent Building Society. The venerable blather-
skite, said Purves, was honest enough but Ins
capacity, beyond understanding a volume o
Burns, was entirely questionable. The capacity
of any man who idolises Bobbie Burns is open to
grave suspicion. However, this is blasphemy
enough for the present.
After all, it seems possible that Harmy
Booth dropped upon his son Ballington for extrava-
gance, or carelessness, in managing the funds or
the American “corps.” The hallelujah officers
over there were found to be living fatter lives
than their redeemed brethren in other parts ol the
world. Some had managed to build houses, one
kept a carriage, and nearly all of them rode their
own safety bikes. Which accounts for their
loyalty to Ballington B.
Nat. Kinsman, the Melbourne auctioneer-
parson who holds the marrying “record,” was 73
in February. He still ties knots, but owing to the
inroads of advertising blackleg rivals, who do the
deed at sweating prices, his trade has much fallen
off. He conducts a Sunday-school and two ser-
vices each Sabbath. His' marriage fee was
witnesses provided, and “my photo, in my gown
with a blessing and a circular, not, as currently
believed, 2s. 6d. and under. Men employed at a
neighboring undertaker’s used to act as witnesses,
for which they were paid by the priest of Hymen.
As many as seven coupled have been on Kinsman’s
premises at one time awaiting their turn.
Mining-speculator Ziman’s late offer of
<£IOJO towards the cost of building a battleship for
the British navy is not the first, nor by any means
the largest offer of that sort, made in these regions.
An “old hand,” who had been transported for
life, afterwards received a pardon, and was, in the
thirties, one of the richest men of his day. He
was anxious to take a trip to England, but could
The Late HENRY PARKES.
Photo, by Falk.
not, because the pardon only extended to the
Australia. His desire to see his old home again
was so great that he offered, if the Government of
the day would only permit him to make the visit
to build a frigate, completely arm her, and hand
her over free of cost. His offer was declined.
Mary Meldrum, of Wangaratta, who died
th ® ° th ® r d fy> was a fine old relic of the stirring
goldfields days. Bom at Clare, Ireland, in 1807
she landed in Victoria in ’35, and, five years later’
married a publican. She delighted in telling of
two diggers who once called for 50 bottles of
champagne, when a bullock-driver replied with
an order for 70. These were produced, but a
digger, who topped the lot with an order for 100
could not be supplied owing to shortness of stock!
In those golden times, her hotel-takings often
amounted to LI 50 per day; but she died poor,
after all.
The last M.L. mining-boom (in the seven-
ties) was the boom of the working miner. Now it
is the boom of specs, and promotions. The most
prominent figure in the present upheaval is Mr.
I> Ziman, who, though he does not look like a
giant of speculation, has formed a L 250,000 com-
pany in London, and tackled some huge ventures
on the rugged West Coast, where, with the assist-
ance of experts like Gordon and Parkes, late of
the M.L. Mines Dept., Yankee Phil. Foster and
others, he should, having been early in the field
and deliberate in his ventures, make money. Mr.
Ziman was born in ’OO in “the fair land of
Poland.” In ’BO he made for South Africa, where
he speculated in produce, ostrich feathers, gold,
and so on, until the glamour of M. L. came over him.
There he bonded for a little money down such mines
as the Globe, Progress, Wealth of Nations, Sed-
don, Cadman, Salisbury, Welcome, Golden Fleece
D. ZIMAN, who is on top of the M.L. West
Coast gold boom.
Photo, by Wrigglesworth & Binns.
&c., &c. With the option in his pocket, he landed
in London, reached the purses of the Roths-
children, the late Baron Hirsch, and others who
follow these leaders, so that the Consolidated
G.M. and Exploration Co., of M.L., with a
quarter of a million for a start, soon laid hands
upon whole areas of West Coast properties. The
pick of experts were obtained, others were bought
out of the Govt, service—and now Mr. Ziman has
taken up his residence at the centre of his field of
operations.

One Alderman Leggatt, of Kiama (N.S.W.),
rose up the other day in defence of the British
flag, a,nd if he doesn’t get a K.C.M.G.-ship there
is no justice left in this world. Leggatt’s trouble
was over the big painted advt. of Hixson’s
Conqueror tobacco, which represents one vessel
being sunk by another, and going down with
her flag flying. Leggatt demanded vociferously
that this sort of thing should be squashed
with the hoof of patriotism. “It is an insult to
the people,” said the Kiama loyalist; “the idea of
a British frigate being sunk by an American. It
is an insult to Us Britishers. An American never
sunk a British frigate yet.” Evidently they don’t
study history much at Kiama, and never heard of
the days when Yankee cruisers practically block-
aded Liverpool, and raged up and down the
.English Channel, and. captured two British war-
ships in the German Ocean, and otherwise made
themselves unpleasant. It is painful to record,
too, tha/b Leggatt boiled over at Kiama to no pur-
pose. The Blow-Hole municipality wants all the
advertising money it can get, and unless Alderman
Leggatt demolishes the publication with an axe in
the night it is going to remain. The most annoy-
aii Y as that the picture represented
the American ship Alabama being sunk by the
American ship Kearsage, and the nag which poor
Leggatt was raving about wasn’t the British flag
at all (Leggatt evidently doesn’t know his beloved
ag when he sees it), and the co. never attempted
to allege anything detrimental to Leggatt’s mother
country, and the whole row started with a lamen-
table mistake.


The branch of Prof. Loisette’s memory
system established in Sydney will be carried on
ff usual, notwithstanding the reported death of
the .Professor, which has not as yet been con-
nrmed. All classes throughout the colonies are
now enrolled amongst the pupils, and those who
are faithfully carrying out the course of training
prescribed have improved not only in their memo-
ries, but, generally speaking, in all their mental
qualities as well.*

T. B. Browne’s “ Advertiser’s ABC for
1896 ” contains a great mass of information con-
cerning newspapers and periodicals in all parts of
the world. The number now on the globe’s list
appears, at a rough estimate, to be 3,416,878 —and
exactly 3,416,878 of these, more or less, profess to
be “ the oldest established paper ” or to have “the
largest circulation in the- district,” and proceed to
smear the wary advertiser with the stickiest kind
of birdlime. Apparently, the only time when a
paper is not the “ best possible medium for adver-
tisers ” is a little time before it is born and five
minutes after it is dead.*

Melb. “ Memo, for the statisticians.
Somebody tells me the nuns here only live six
years, on an average alter entering the convent,
and certainly we rarely see an elderly one.”
In Michigan State prison the convicts are
allowed to keep singing birds, such as canaries, in
their cells for company; also to raise birds for
sale, the proceeds being banked and handed over
on release. To-day there are over 600 canaries
alone in the prison, and the privilege is affirmed
to have much diminished outbreaks and improved
general conduct.

securities in the Colonies or Great Britain ; undertakes

Public Notices.
■\TOTICE is hereby given that it is the intention of
-iA BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, of Sydney, in the
colony of New South Wales, Member of the Institute of
Civil Engineers, Esquire, to apply to the Parliament of
New South Wales during the next session for a Bill to
authorise the said BENJAMIN CRISPIN SIMPSON, or
his Assigns, to construct a Bridge and Approaches thereto
between the South and North Shores of Port Jackson, the
said Bridge and Approaches to commence at a point in
Cumberland-street, in the city of Sydney, about thirty-
three chains fifty links north from the intersection of
Cumberland-street with Charlotte-place, in the city of
Sydney, to Dawes Point, thence to and crossing the Waters
of Port Jackson to Milson’s Point, North Sydney, thence
to and terminating at a point in Alfred-street, about ten
chains fifty links north of the intersection of Dind-street
and the said Alfred-street, in the borough of North
Sydney ; to authorise the building over and diversion of
streets or roads; to authorise the appropriation or use of
certain lands required for or in connection with the carry-
ing out of the said work ; to make provision for compen-
sating the owners of and others interested in such lands;
to authorise the imposition of tolls on persons using the
said bridge ; to confer powers for the making and enforce-
ment of bylaw's ; and for all other purposes which may be
incidental thereto. Dated at Sydney this 13th day of
April, 1896.
LEONARD GIBSON, 92 Pitt-street, Sydney, Solicitor
or the Bill.

The Colonial Bank of Australasia

THE SIXTH REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS
Oi the Colonial Bank of Australasia Limited, presented to the Shareholders at the Sixth Ordinary
m-Mating, held at the Bank, 126 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, at noon on
THURSDAY, 3uth ABRIL, 1896.
Report.
a The directors beg to submit to the shareholders their Sixth Report, with a Balance-sheet and
Statement of Profit and Loss, for the half-year ended 31st March, duly audited.
Alter providing for expenses of management, interest accrued on deposits, rebate on bills current,
and tax on note circulation, the net profit, including the balance brought forward from previous half-
to =£3587 165., which the directors recommend be carried forward to next half-year.
During the half-year under review the directors have experienced great difficulty in finding sound
and profitable investments for the employment of the bank’s funds, and the profits of the bank have
been materially affected by the low rates ruling for money.
The amendment of the Companies Act not having been passed by Parliament, the losses referred
m Previous reports cannot yet be provided for by writing off a portion of the capital.
1 he SIXTH ORDINARY GLNLRAL MEETING of Shareholders will be held at the head
lO Company, 126 Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, on THURSDAY, the 30th day of APRIL,
1896, at noon, By order of the Board.
„ „ SELBY PAXTON, General Manager.
Melbourne, 23rd April, 1896.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Australian Fourteen did not sign agreements
nntil after the Cuzoo left Albany. The agreements are
not the same, and it will take the manager most of his
time to keep peaoe. The soreness on previous trips has
almost invariably arisen, in the first instance, through cer-
tain players getting a bigger share of the profits than
others. They all do the same work, and if one or two of
the “ lesser paid ’’ happen to “ come off,” as they usually
do, the trouble begins.
Harry Trott takes the least exercise of the team
©n board ship, when travelling. M'Kihbin seems to he
always at it, and plays the usual round of deck-games
from morn till night, and, even when going from one part
of the boat to another, swings on ropes and crossbars like
a monkey.
Bye : “ The trouble re the selection of the Aus-
tralian Eleven was caused primarily by the Cricket Council.
The Council takes no part in financing the tour, that part of
the business being left to the manager, and yet this
utterly irresponsible body claims the right to ratify
agreements and dictate rules down to the veriest details.
The contract signed by the members of the team had to
be approved by the Counoil, and the question naturally
arose—“ What have they got to do with it \ We’re taking
ourselves to England. The next Australian Eleven
should be selected and managed by those who find the
cash. It will save a lot of trouble.” On the other hand,
it is selection and endorsement by a representative body
like the Cricket Council that make “ the Australian
Eleven." “ The Australian Eleven ” isn’t any self-satisfied
dozen of cricketers who agree to go on the rampage after
British cash.
Eady, who (excepting the Test-match) has never
played in fiMi,-class cricket, owes his inclusion in the
team to the fact that he represents a fourth province,
and is “ presentable socially.” Harry, undoubtedly a
better cricketer, was practically “decided against” from
the very date of his selection because he was not likely to
shape well in bell-topper and frock-coat society.
The present English tour is likely to be handled
by Harry Musgrove on far better business principles than
preceding ones. At least' one of the recent tours is not
settled financially yet—and not likely to be.
The match usually played at Colombo by, the
Australian cricketers is really only an elaborate picnic for
the Eleven, when members sign for any drinks they want
and eat a tremendous luncheon—the whole carouse being
paid for by the Colombo Englishman. From a cricketing
point of view, the match is valueless.
» * * *
Carlton’s captain, “ Jacky/ Worrall, by his
phenomenal average of, 108 runs per innings, wins Wil-
liamson and Musgrove’s crieket-trophy. The permanent
possessor had to head pennant cricket batting averages
for at least two years. Worrall has done'this and secured
the trophy, .with a life free-pass*to all the firm’s theatres.

Rumors of an offer to Jack Harry by a wealthy
Adelaide cricket enthusiast, whicFUmay lead to his resi-
dence in Adelaide when he returns to Australia.
* * * *
Vic. football grows deader every year: gate-
money, cronk professionals and the cycling craze. A few
years back Melb. April papers reeked with particulars of
movements of the big player, the promising junibr, and
Billy the Long-drop. That seems a nightmare of the
pait.
• • * •
Baseball is death-knelled down South. The
M.C.C. is abandoning it this winter, and, along with other
clubs, taking up lacrosse.
* * * *
How Adelaide football declines in popularity !
Nor-wood club receipts for’94 were £399 os. Bd., and for’9s
£l7O 12s. lid. —a difference of £228 7s. 9d. All the Ade-
laide clubs are in debt.
* * *
The Sydney Polo Club’s gymkhana at Kensing-
ton last Saturday provided the usual amount of smash.
Last year a steeplechase was run, which resulted' in the
laying out and severe injury of most of the riders.
This year there was no chase, but the management, know-
ing that the spectators wanted blood for their money, put
on a-“ Gretna Green race” —an affair supposed to repre-
sent a race between a lot of eloping couples and in which
some of the competitors put on women’s clothes of various
degrees of loudness and bad taste, and started to race
over hurdles. To make the thing more exciting, two or
three men from the barracks were introduced as police-
men to catch the eloping pairs, and either under instruc-
tions or from sheer native idiocy they rode across the
competitors who were racing at the hurdles. This
naturally led to trouble, and after some narrow escapes of
a smash “Banjo” Paterson, in dodging one of these
“ policemen,” as they were called, came full speed
into 1 Veterinary Surgeon Captain W. Scott, with the
result that both horses were thrown and Capt. Scott was
Stunned and severely-shaken ; the Banjo’s horse was badly
hurt, but the rider escaped scot-free, though another
competitor rode over him at full gallop as he struggled to
his feet after the first smash. This incident imparted a
pleasing variety to an otherwise insipid entertainment.
The race was ordered to be run over again, as the “ police-
men” were introduced without the sanction of the
committee or the knowledge of the competitors as
a whole, though some of them afterwards .confessed
they were in the know.” In fact, after the
race, no one seemed anxious to claim the credit
of this dangerous and stupid bungle. The “ non-
bloodshed ” events resulted satisfactorily, “ Tom ”
Watson winning the polo ball race, as he always does,
while Paterson secured the cigar and umbrella. Brock
■Won the Cup and tandem and costume races, and Dr. Fox
and Mr. Oulley, of Narandera, accounted for the other
two events. Then the spectators, who were mostly of the
very Mite, retired to their ancestral castles, saying that it
had been an excellent day’s sport except for the accident
to poor dear Captain Scott; hut so long as the polo com-
mittee can guarantee plenty smash and injury to horses
and men at their races they will never lack a fashionable
audience.
* » » *
M.L. Amateur Athletic Association has decreed
that a chest of, drawers isn’t a legitimate prize. There
mayn’t be much poetry about chests of drawers, hut they
are infinitely preferable to worthless metal cups, obtain-
able cheap when bought by the cartload from the local
ironmonger.
« * * *
Frank Geale, the Tas. axeman who chopped
second to Tom Reeves for the championship last Nov., is
to chop Curran, of N.5.W.., for £lOO on May 24. As
Geale was only beaten by a few splinters by champion
Reeves, the corns.talk‘ win be a wonder if he beats the
slab-sided Tasmanian. In chopping matches, big stakes
seem to be the vogue. It’s one of the very few games
in which the favorite can’t run a bye, or ease up when
no one’s looking.
• * • *
If Fitzsimmons has nous he’ll lie back and take
a long breath. Jonnel and Gentleman Jim didn’t fight
for years when they bossed the ring, and a credulous
’Murkan public stood by them. Corbett, by the way, is
allegedly eager to get at Fitz. When champion a few
years ago he' was equally anxious to publicly batter
Mitchell. He knew Mitchell was in gaol at the time.
Articles have been signed in Wellington (M.L.)
for a match between Owen Higgins and Jim Pettengejl to
decide the M.L. boxing championship. There are about
a doz. champs, in M.L. Emigrant Starlight and Charley
Richardson are among the claimants.
* * * *
The Megson-Lewis benefit cycle-meeting at
Sydney last Saturday was a great success ; honors were
divided between Megson, Payne, and the ever-constant
Martin. The League’s arrangements were not up to the
usual mark—in fact, the show progressed at times with
the speed and motion of an overladen hearse.
In the final of the lap dash (a most interesting
Innovation, by the way), Martin went to the front at
gisboi-oraok, and came home a length from Parsons. The
Tank, again showed himself the best Judge and strongest
tmd gamest rider. Compared with other riders, it might
he sard that Martin has brains in his feet.
The race for boys under 12 was a dull and flat
proceeding relieved by protests. One under-12 boy was
protested against because he was a father, a second for
not having shaved for two days, a third for being above
the age of consent.
The motocycle, judged by its exhibition, is a
fearful vibratory engine, riding which should be tanta-
mount to riding a lame camel whose hump is a galvanic
battery.
Somebody put two charges in the (Chinese)
starter’s gun, with the result that the starter-nearly died
through shock to the system. Recovering, he told a
story meant to be apropos :—“Lunce, lahlah timit lung
fellah he like it to be a sholjah ; so he go join em army.
He out on dlill wit his legiment. Each time charge lung
fellah put in, each time offisah call it fi-ah ! Each time
offisah find lung fellah cove his report not go off ! Offisah
no hear it bom! ‘Stand ow ! foah yars !’ offisah say.
Timit lung fellah he stan ow. ‘ Fiah ! ’ offisah call it out.
Three charge in gun lung fellah charge go off all wunce,
knock im lung fellah head over heels. Offisah tink do im
good. But lung fellah cove he lub on much oindmon.”

Martin, scientifically paced by M‘Lean (the 10-
miles championship), added a few more (lols. to his ever-
growing pile, by winning the Melb. 8-Hours’ wheel
race. That pub. in America must be nearly within the
Plugger’s reach now. Judging by his recent perform-
ances, Martin should be quite able to preserve order in
his bar, unless Johnell Sullivan happens to drop in for a
social evening.

Parsons Melb. benefit realised £250. His visit
to Europe will be a honeymoon trip, after all.
The bride-to-be is Miss Bella Russell little Bella
Russell well-known to all Australian playgoers
as the tearful and offensively-honest newsboy in many
Adelphi melodramas. Miss Bella was born to the stage,
being a daughter of Nellie Greenless, the crack danseuse
of the Melb. boards 10 or 15 years ago.

Vic. Wheelmen’s League intends “enforcing
decency on the track,” and, to begin, interdicted the use
of woven knickerbockers, which are alleged to show up
the shape too much. A deadly stab at feminine interest.
A prominent cyclist is credited with being in
league! with the bookmakers, a rumor given color to
by the factUhat when he scooped the pool at a recent
meeting the penc.illers would not iaj' a penny against
him. As a manly sport cycle-racing is rapidly becoming
flyblown—like other sports where the man with the bag
gets a footing.
* * * *
“ He won’t win another race this season ; and
yet he’ll piek up rnoro\ in@ney than any of us ;” said one
racing cyclist of another two months ago. Since then
that other has paced several men to victory, finishing
ignominiously himself. One case was particularly glar-
ing ; even the easily-hoodwinked public commenting.

* * * *
From a spectacular standpoint the recent Christ-
church (M.L.) ladies’ cycling carnival wasn’t a success.
The bloomered talent were “ trained too fine,” and the
back view, when racing, was remembersome. A solitary
fair, fat, and fancied damsel was the only exception. She
was apparently fed up for the occasion, but her wind
gave out. The spectators were very candid, too : “ Take
yer machine home, Sis, and pad out next time.” “ Look
at her, the wish-bone of a turkey dolled up in pants.”
When people get the particulars of a woman’s anatomy,
it’s too late t@ start “ putting on flesh.”
• • • •
The Rev. Rentoul, ye ken, Presbyterian meenis-
ter of Melbourne : “I dislike to see a young man astride
his bicycle on a Sabbath morning, thinking of nothing
but his own ugly self and his pleasures.” Another enemy
of the Plate. “His own ugly self”! Surely even a bi-
cyclist is the image of his Maker.
* » * *
Memmott—who, by the way, seems to .ha we
asserted a supremacy over Stevenson, the South African
champion—talks of returning from London to Australia
this winter. Melbourne is dead, as regards professional
billiard-playing, so, if Memmott is to work up a boom, it
will have to be in Sydney, where Gray (another Victorian),
James, and veteran Harry Evans are available for exhibi-
tion matches. Two years in London, among the masters
of the game, must have improved Memmott considerably,
and he ought to be able to teach a lot more than he knew
himself when he won the Australian championship.
* * * »
With all this New Woman, where is the
billiardiste ?
* * * *
Upon the discovery of the spot-stroke in billiards
all-round play became a thing of the past, and to make
the game of interest it was found necessary to “ bar ” the
monotonous spot. As with billiardists so with glove-
fighters. The discovery that a man has a chin has re-
volutionised the game, and in place of the old-time hun-
dred Founds of blood apd thunder the average glove-fight
now consists of a round and a half, until one of the
fighters finds his opponent’s chin. To remedy the evil
and return to the good old Mays of gore—and gate—there
is.but one course open—to follow in the wake of billiards,
and bar the “ chin stroke.”
* » * »
Murrumbidgee Turf Club’s method of keeping
the bailiff out. It proposes asking eyery surrounding
breeder to give (gratis) a horse or two to be raffled at a
shilling a ticket. The scheme should find favor with
creditors if not with breeders.
* * * *
V.R.C. committee had a backed-up =£2o appeal
the other day from a decision given by its stewards. The
thing was ridiculous on the face of it, the stewards being
the committee also. So that an owner disagreeing with a
metropolitan club has no redress. The V.R.C. uphold or
vary appeals from other clubs, including Caulfield ; in
their own cases they assume infallibility.
Adelaide bookmakers were hit so heavily over
recent meetings—especially Onkaparinga—that only three
or four have so far met their engagements. Better to be
an organ-grinder than an Adelaide “ book ” these.-days.
* * * *
A Rand wick lady visitor, an attractive moving
picture, with cobwebby tresses of shimmering gold,
approached a noted jock, the other day and told him
she’d drawn his mount in a sweep. Mr. Colors thought
it was Tatt.’s sweep, but it turned out to be a half-crown
swindle. Nathless, she hoped “ he’d lay back-until the
finish and then come up and win.” It sounded like telling
a barber to go and get pis ’air cut.
* * * *
“Charlie” Morris’s hunting champion, Fair-
field, has never fallen at a fenee. He won scores of hunting-
competitions last year, and within the last three weeks
has cleared £l5O in show prizes.
* * * *
Tatt.’s sweep on Canterbury handicap had the
customary interesting effect; 18 started out of 36. Had
the expected “ bit to nix ” been forthcoming the whole
crowd would have probably been saddled. One thing
about a sweep-race is that neddies seem to try much
harder than usual—if there’s nothing more tempting in
the immediate future.

Mr. Herbert Creek, who spent some time in
Australia, and who recently married one of the wealtny
Misses Dugdale, met with a fatal accident whilst h anting
with the Cotswolds a few days ago, writes The Bulletin s
London correspondent. He has been in *!»
an Australian buckjnmping-saddle, to which 'Sheath
attributed, for, when his horse fell, he could not clear
himself of the large pads, and the horse rolled on him.
* * •
Two men engaged in the business of “inform-
ing ” on Melb. tote-keepers have been committed tor per-
iurv. They carried on the industry under aliases, as the
“informer"” is, somehow, not looked upon with special
favor, and in giving their evidence they swore to their
names in the usual way. Ikey Mo. discovered the state ot
affairs, after a very long investigation, and informed on
them, with the above disastrous results.
A Sydney trotter ‘ ‘ ringing-in ” gang has been
active in M.L. lately, but its game is being checked,
and suspects are passed out weekly. Officials regularly
watch the arrival of vessels North and South, taking a
descriptive note of every suspicious-looking crowd and
their prads.
» * * *
A Tote-bill is on the tapis for next Yic. session.
If so, the ho'y and unholy combination will have to once
again “ organise.” Mention the subject to. a frightened
bookie now, and he assumes the gloomy air of the last
man at a funeral.
* * * *
It is likely that should the tote continue to
reign in M.L. an attempt will be made to deal with the
double-string business. An owner running two in a race
is invariably accused of winning with the outsider in pre-
ference to the favorite for the sake of the div., whether
such was his intention or not. Under the French method
the pair would be bracketed, and losers paid if the stable-
mate won.
* * * *
It is said that the ill-starred owner of the yacht
sunk in Port Phillip Bay on Easter Monday was presented
with the boat by his uncle to keep him away from the race-
course.
» * * *
Parsons and Harris are rather neatly-footed, but
have each a fair share of hand. Porta does not take
“ladies’ two” in footwear, but Martin’s extremities are most
generous, and the late Chang would probably/have found
little difficulty in wearing either his boots or gloves—pre-
suming, of course, that “ Plugger Bill ” ever descended to
the extravagant frivolity of gloves.
• * * •
At Bathurst (N.S.W.) show, a local horse
(for the special edification of Gov. Hampden) bucked
himself clean out of the saddle, the rider—W. Wright, of
Yetholme—holding his seat all the time. Which perform-
ance will probably be reported in Hingland as tending to
tighten “ the silken bonds.”


THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H- Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newnran is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*

Capt. Rossi, noted in N.S.W. public
memory as the layman champion in the long and
heated Goulburn Anglican Cathedral dispute, now
claims to be Comte do Rossi, and is acting aceord-
mgly. The “ berlud ” “ runs back to the 9th cen-
tury,” says le Comte, modestly, “ and then Louis
XYI. of France confirmed the title later.” The
title has fallen on lo Comte “ by a detour,” to use
his own phrase. “It isn’t like a title by purchase,
you know,” adds le Comte ; “ and, when you get
it by right, it’s something, isn’t it ? ” Then Count
Rossi relates how the French Government (Re-
publican !) had instructed its Consul-General in
Australia to discover the noble Capting’s where-
abouts, and declare to him his sang pur.
Re Prenjier Reid’s recent shark-catching
exploit, it is related that the Premier arranged
with Proctor, the professional shark-slayer, to go
down Sydney harbor and kill the next shark which
should take one of Proctor’s buoyed baits. Early
on Friday week Proctor saw the buoy dipping, and
found a 12ft. 4in. monster hooked. So as to make
it a short and easy job for the Premier, the shark-
expert reduced the length of chain, which meant
less play for the fish and an earlier death. Mr.
Reid was telephoned for same day, but, as he
ouuld not find leisure till mid-day on Saturday,
the soldiers in camp were convulsed at the sight of
Georgey Porgey going out to kill a dead shark.
From the boatshed, where the men were messing,
the vengeful G. H. could be seen, harpoon in hand,
“dealing it out” to a lifeless fish. When the
Premier returned with his victim, the soldiers
were about to greet him with three ringing cheers,
but were cautioned by the eye of “ discipline.”

The idea of utilising native cats to destroy
rabbits was tried lately near Broken Hill, with
the result that bunny and the feline fraternised
most amiably. A farmer in that locality points to
a big burrow which for long protected a choice
assortment : a ’guana, a native cat, a little
mongrel fox-terrier slut with four pups, and a
family of rabbits.
The other day, a deputation of three
Cabinet Ministers, three M’s.L.A., and three
municipal councillors brought down all the way
from Ballarat, applied to Vic. P.M.G. Duffy to
make the Ballarat postmaster live on the post-
office premises, so that he could be roused out at
dead of night by anyone who wanted a penny
stamp. Great inconvenience, it seems, is caused
by the present arrangement to people who want a
stamp at night or in the small hours of the morn-
ing. Australia has got the deputation craze badly.
Australian saltbush is being profusely sown
in the alkali districts of the S.W. States ofU.S. A.,
where formerly vegetation was totally unknown.
It grows and spreads splendidly. It has also
been thoroughly acclimatised in French North
Africa, and by the English from Cape Town to
the tropic of Capricorn. Australian pastoralists
in poor country might well follow up the hint,
and systematically sow saltbush where grass is
weak. The African and American sowings are
the direct results of Baron von Mueller’s advice, to
which also is due the gift of the eucalyptus to
swampy Southern Europe, and of the tan-wattles
and gums to Yankeeland.
The Argus, dilating on the iniquities of
Melbourne J.P.-ism, always points its moral and
adorns its tale with the “ Collingwood Trades
Hall affair,” as it prefers to call the Shelmerdene
case, in which a number of Radical Js.P. —avowed
partisans—turned up to have a say on a Bench
packed with avowed capitalistic sympathisers.
There are lots of more awful examples, but the
Argus flourishes that instance of unionistic
abomination for all it’s worth. The Trades Hall
can count on that infamy being still actively on
the job, illustrating the low, immoral, and corrupt
nature of radicals generally, when Baxter and
Rapiport are faded and forgotten, and when the
Bird who fouled the magisterial nest has fallen
out of vogue as a journalistic bogey.
Dear Bulletin, — Re contribution by
“How’s That.” The omission of one letter from
a name may entirely alter its signification. The
Holey Box—a camp about midway between Hay
and Wilcannia —received its appellation, in the
days when the world was wide, from the excep-
tional size and depth of the crab-holes in the box.
“ Box,”of course, signified a clump of swamp box,
—Riverine taciturnity admitting of no superfluous
words. The last of the box-trees at this camp —
there were only a few to begin with—was used up
by carriers and drovers about 18 years ago ; hence,
the title has become a mere meaningless, anti-
quated figure of speech, like “ Gracious Majesty ”
or “ Most Reverend.” Still, it is historic, whereas
“ Holy Box,” as spelled by “ How’s That,” seems
to smack of Orange ignorance and wanton ir-
reverence.—Tom Collins.

Mr. Button writes later: —“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your

A Wonderful Cure.
Herr Rassmussen. August, 1894.
Dear Sir, —In the year of 1871 I was eight days
in the water sheep-washing, and contracted rheu-
matism and stiffness in the joints, arid have tried
all advertised medicines. When I came to Sydney
I had a great difficulty in walking, and went to
the Turkish baths, which did no good. Passing
your window I saw a testimonial from Mr. E.
W. O’Sullivan, M.P., with the same complaint as
I had, which led me to try your Remedies. On
Thursday before Good Friday I took the first of
your Pills ; since then I have used 4 boxes Speoial
Powerful Alfaline Vitality Pills, 3 boxes Special
Powerful Alfaline Rheumatic Pills, with Liver
and Kidney Pills, and free use of your Alfaline
Eucalyptus Oil, and I am now, with the blessing
of God, as well in health as I was 23 years ago.
I can with great confidence recommend your
Remedies to anyone suffering from the same com-
plaint. May you long be spared under God to
be a blessing to suffering humanity.
I remain, dear sir, yours very gratefully,
JOHN KEAY.
Witness : Edward Hardy, proprietor Town
Hall Coffee Palace, 521 George-street.

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of GGOft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
R 5, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
The Meudon tanneries, in which skins of
guillotined aristocrats, male and female, were
tanned into leather “ resembling chamois,” subse-
quently converted into breeches and gloves for
good patriots and patriotesses, were among the
most gruesome phenomena of the French Revolu-
tion. Well, something of the sort, in a small
way, still exists to prove that human hide-tanning
is not a lost art. The writer was recently shown
a piece of skin, soft and flexible, which had been
tanned for a medical student by a Richmond
(Vic.) tanner. The tanner, asked if he did much
work of this ghastly kind, replied that he did not,
but that there was a man he knew who did plenty
of it. Then he related the history of the piece of
corpse-skin held in his hand. “ When it was sent
to me to tan,” he said, “ I couldn’t make out what
sort of skin it was. First I thought it was pig-
skin, but when I compared it with some pig-skin
I had I saw that this (tapping the leather) was
much finer in grain. Then I thought that it
might be elephant-skin ” —here he explained
that the skin of the inside of the fore-thighs
of an elephant is extremely thin and fine.
“ But I found out it wasn’t that,” he went on,”
“ and didn’t try to guess any more, but tanned it.
The medicai student who sent it to me called the
next day, and told me it had been taken from the
left breast and part of the side of a woman, who
had died in the hospital. He said he was going
to have it made up into a pocket-book and
tobacco-pouch.” The writer subsequent’y met
the man who made a specialty of this shudder-
some business. He was affable and communica-
tive, and said that he had had in his possession
some time back tanned pieces of the skins of two
women—one old, the other young. He had
divided the latter amongst his friends, and had
none left, but he still had nearly the whole piece
of the old woman’s epidermis. “ Nobody wanted
it,” he said ; “ they all went for the skin of the
young party.” Sentiment, it seems, can survive
death, and even tanning. These facts are curious,
if not cheerful, and show that no man ever dies
really destitute. His skin is always an asset of
some value, which, if he likes to do a graceful
thing, he can bequeath to his friends.

The Recording Angel’s Tear.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man s face was neither heated nor angry.
He had cold, scrutinising eyes and a straight,
hard mouth. There was a line showing irritation
between his eyebrows.
“I had rather hear no more,” he said quietly as
the girl paused for a moment. “ The past is done
with. It was not so pleasant that I should wish
to hear it recalled. May we consider this inter-
view at an end?” His hand went towards the
befl. I he girl was almost baffled, but she made
one last effort. She had pleaded with tear-dimmed
eyes, and she trembled in every limb.
Oh, can nothing move you ? ” she cried tremu-
lously. She was such a child when she married
you, and you were always so cold. She couldn’t
live without love, and so she fell, but she always
loved you. She has repented for years. She is
dying, and night and day she moans for you, to
see you and ask your forgiveness. Oh, if you ever
cared for her, if you have not a heart of stone
send some message if you will not see her. She is
dying.”
. The man turned on her sharply. “Dying
is she. and you want a message ? Tell your
he said slowly, “that I would to God
she had died before I saw her, before she was able
to ruin my home and drag my name in the dirt.
Damn her!” he said with sudden violence, “let
her die ! ”
Outside, the night stillness was broken by the
hoarse, jarring shriek of the flying-foxes, the
snapping of twigs and tearing of leaves as they
threw themselves among the branches of the fig-
trees, and the gurgle—like dropping water—of
innumerable frogs. Inside, there was a buzz of
mosquitoes and the rattling, labored breath of a
dying woman. Her body was already dead ; only
the head turned restlessly from side to side, and
the eyes in their agony told of the mind that would
not die. The question in those eyes, as the door
creaked and opened, was so imperative that the
incomer could not choose but answer. Her hesi-
tation ceased as she saw the restless longing.
“ He was away from home,” she said, trying to
put truth into her voice. She drew aside the
mosquito-net and fanned the hot, clammy face ;
but the question still remained in the dying eyes.
“Yes, dear,” she went on, soothingly, “of course,
I wrote as you asked me to. He will get the letter
to-morrow, and then he will come.” But her heart
said, “ Before then, please God, she will be dead.”
The eyes closed, and the breath came pantingly
through the discolored lips. The hands, lying
powerless on the sheet, were blue and swollen.
The hours passed wearily by. The doctor came in
silently, saw the mark of Death’s finger-touch, and
left her undisturbed. The flying-foxes were silent
at last. A large moth flew in and flapped its
wings along the papered ceiling, and at last the
eyes opened.
“Is he coming? I can’t wait. Won’t he come?”
The girl bent over her.
“He is coming-,” she said, in an agony of sym-
pathy. “ He will soon be here.”
“ Will he—ever—forgive? ”
“Yes, that was his message; he forgives and
loves you still.”
“Loves?”
“Yes, he has always loved you.”
The end was very near, and the eyes were
glazing over. The watcher braced herself for the
last lie. Very tenderly she raised the helpless
figure.
“He is coming, dear,” she whispered; “he is
coming—I can hear his step upon the stair,” and
there was a smile of great joy on the dead face as
she gently lowered it on to the pillow.

Melb. Age on the recent military man-
oeuvres:—
It is worthy of note that the horse artillery got their
guns to the top of the hill without the carriages falling to
pieces. These guns are in a pitiable condition through
?I d age ! and itj if t really not safe to move them. The guns
themselves are obsolete, while the carriages and limbers
are not merely out of date, but are literally tumbling into
fragments through decay. Five of the axle-boxes were
thrown off when the guns were fired, their iron brackets
being broken, while the screws simply drew out of the
ho,es - On the way back to camp to-day another axle-box
was . found lying-in the road having fallen off the gun-
carriage and never been missed. There is only one of the
three guns now intact, and the condition of all of them is
seriously defective, the woodwork being rotten from end
to end. If the Defence department can arrange to find
the money, it would be a great gain to the defence of the
00untr y to provide Maxim guns instead of these ram
shackle weapons ’ and this could be done at no great cost
The suggestion that pastoral tenants of
the Crown should be made to pay rent based, not
on the fair carrying capacity of the country, but
on the number of stock actually carried, has some
sound, hard sense about it. If carried into effect
it would, at any rate, not put a premium on over-
stocking. As matters stand, the chances are that
in rabbit-infested country quite as much damage
has in many instances been done by over-stocking
as by the vermin, and now the State is required to
suffer through a rent-reduction for the deliberate
spoliation of its own property. The arrangements
made between the benefiting occupier and the
mortgage co. in almost every case tend to over-
stocking and to the consequent permanent damage
or ruin of tracts of country which presently, be-
coming wholly unprofitable to all concerned, are
abandoned—and that is the worst possible thing
that can happen both to the property and to the
neighborhood.
The municipal pub. has been tried success-
fully at Elan, near Birmingham (Eng.). It is
closed on Sundays ; opened on other days only
from noon to 1.30 p.m., and 5.30 to 9 p.m. Three
quarts of beer, administered in instalments, are the
maximum daily allowance per customer. The nett
profits for the first half-year were £l4O (Elan is
only a village), and were devoted to the establish-
ment of a reading and recreation-room. Whole-
some liquor has been dispensed, and drunkenness
minimised.
It is sad to think that ‘ ‘ our literature
can never be graced by the idiom of the indigenous
digger, for the simple but convincing reason that
his pet phrases and most characteristic appella-
tions are altogether too gorgeous for publication.
Sad to say, it is the Australian-born miner who is
mainly responsible for the existing vocabulary,
and by him everything, from a dumpling to a plug
of dynamite, is designated by a term that is utterly
impossible in print. The alleged Australian ad-
jective lost its effect by use long ago, and is now
never used by a convincing linguist, excepting
in tow of a more lurid and spirit-stirring
blast. The miner swears more in friendship than
in anger, and the man cursed and condemned from
eyes to ankles knows that the : procession is in his
honor, and is duly grateful.
In Southern Queensland there are Ger-
mans who positively buy nothing but leather and
cloth. Their bread they make from their own
corn; their meat is raised on the farm —some-
times pork, sometimes wallaby; they raise their
own tobacco; grow their own coffee, and some-
times the sugar also with which it is sweetened.
The driver of the Echuca - Koondrook
(Vic.) coach picked up on the track the other day
a pair of handcuffs, evidently dropped by a police-
trooper. Out of curiosity he tried them on to see
if they fitted. They did, and so well that he
couldn’t undo them, even after applying axle-
grease to his wrists, and he was finally obliged
to drive, handcuffed, to the. next township, where
the lockup-keeper released him.
Dear Bulletin, —Re aboriginal cave-
paintings. About 65 miles from Northam, W. A.,
is a granite cave in which I counted 17 hand-pic-
tures perfectly delineated, and of the size of an
ordinary human hand. They appear to be in-
grained into the stone. Singularly enough, they
all represent the left hand. I have made many
enquiries of the natives as to the reason of these
paintings, but found them always either unable or
unwilling to tell.—J. Price, Kalgoorlie, W.A.
E. D. Williams, member for Castlemaine,
tried to be too pleasant on the subject of honorary
magistrates. He said that although he had been
long associated with the fraternity he could only
call to mind two cases where justices had been
swayed by wrong motives. This sounded very
gratifying until somebody suggested that Williams
didn’t associate with any beaks outside of Castle-
maine, and therefore he was casting aspersions
upon a pair of local men. At latest dates the one-
horse town was agitating for names, dates, and
other details, and Williams, M.L.A., was very
sorry he spoke.
Alexander Collie, one of the grandest
swindlers of his generation, died not long since in
America. The Collie firm failed in London about
1875 for a small trifle of £3,000,000, after dis-
counting =£1,600,000 worth of bogus bills with
various banks. Alexander, the boss man of the
firm, was let out on £ 1000 bail and his own recog-
nisances for ,£lOOO more, the law quite failing to
recognise that tying down a £1, 600,000 swindler
with £lOOO bail was much the same as fastening a
robust elephant with thread. He wisely left per
first steamer, as any reasonable man would have
done under the like circumstances, and never
came back.
Dear Bulletin, —‘ ‘ The poor farmer,
struggling under a heavy mortgage.” Yah !
Why doesn’t someone publish an enquiry into the
proportion of liens due to misfortune, and that
owing to mere recklessness or land-grabbing?
At any rate, about here, five “ cockies ” in ten
badly neglect onion-bed and manure-heap, and
their embarrassment is thus much akin to that of
the boys discovered “ wagging it.” Half the
phrase, at least, is mere “barracking ’ for beer
(or beer’s collaterals). To speak simply, they
struggle because they don’t. (P.S. —Excepting
the offer of State mills, by a blazing Freetrade
Premier, to expiring sugarcanists, and barring
also our Holy Faith, I take this to be the
“ record ” joke of this planet. Pardon this noble
pride.)—J.O., Harden.
Christmas at Home .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Grasshoppers in millions —sun beastly hot—
d the flies ! It’s just like old times.
“ Christmas Eve —and I haven’t been home for six
years. Another half-hour’ll do it now. Wonder
how the old people are getting on. Soon home—
the house in sight. Just over this bit of a rise,
and I’ll see the old place I was born in. Lord !
I’ve been in a good many places since I left it.
Shorn a few sheep in my time. Driven a few
mobs of cattle—seen a bit of hard life on the
Darling—and a bit of harder on the goldfields—
since I last climbed that old woollybutt tree
there. It hasn’t changed much.
“Ah, there it is ! Same old hut; same kitchen ;
same log fence round the garden where the
pumpkins grow. There’s old Polly grazing down
on the flat. She looks pretty poor this Christmas.
Look at her ribs ! And there’s mother with dad’s
hat on looking over the fence. She’ll not know
me, I’ll bet.”
We met. Mother and son, after six years ; and
great was the rejoicing in the little farm-house in
Native Dog Gully. I saw at a glance that things
were going hard with the old folks this year ; and
mother’s lips quivered when she told me that the
drought killed all the crops. I looked out into the
fields ; they were baked up—nothing left.
“ Now’s my time,” thought I, and out came
half my six years’ savings. Then things hummed
in Native Dog Gully. Dad went straight away
and brought back a lot of bottles in mother’s egg-
basket, and joy reigned.
Clinking of glasses ! Whisky ! Wine ! Beer !
Joy ! More joy ! Merry Christmas ! There’s the
dinner-bell! Christmas dinner ! with the dear
old folks at home.
“Dong! Dong! Dong!” rang the night-shift
off. I awoke too late. I looked out of the win-
dow, and saw the day-shift, with billies and tucker,
flocking to the shaft. I went back and sat on the
bed, rubbing my eyes. Where was Christmas
dinner ? Where were mother and dad ?
Yesterday was Christmas day.
