The Sweater.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And shall all men the Sweater meek deride,
And not a voice be raised upon his side ?
Perish the thought! Behold one hireling pen
Shall praise his virtues in the face of men.
He shall not go unsung his doom to meet,
Spurned hell ward by the “wild mob’s million
feet.”
What is a nation’s strength, a nation’s pride ?
Ye fools, it is the Sweater Glorified —
The portly-stomached magnate of Toorak !
Ye are but parasites upon his back.
Your days, your nights are his: your children,
wives,
Blood, sweat, and breath are his —your very lives.
And would ye seek to blight him with vain breath
Whose pedigree is old as Sin and Death ?
When Adam first from Paradise was driv’n
And unto him the stern command was giv’n
To till the earth—a fairer Earth than now—
With bitter sweat of his ancestral brow,
Behold, O foolish, thoughtless working men,
Was Satan not the first of Sweaters then ?
But his descendants show in other guise. . . .
Devout and fat, with upturned pious eyes,
You may in church upon each Sunday note
The Sweater, dressed in glossy broadcloth coat
List’ning to some fine sermon, frank and free,
Upon the Holiness of Poverty.
Is he a sinner ? Say so if you please !
He has the faith of the old French Marquise,
And thinks that God will ponder twice ere He
Will damn a person of his quality.
Why should God damn him ? In the great design
Is he not, too, an instrument divine,
Whereby through pain and anguish men are driv’n
To look from hopeless Earth with hope to Heav’n?
For, lo—ye wicked scoffers, cease your laughter !
His Hell on Earth preserves from Hell hereafter.
“Your wives and children starve?” Ye griping
crew,
What then ? Has he not wife and children, too,
Who must be fed upon the very best
That gold can buy—in raiment rich be dressed,
And all the pleasures that the Earth can give
Enjoy—if they will condescend to live? . . .
Ev’n then they find—with all these at command—
Life harder far than ye can understand.
O gaunt-faced woman, with weak, straining eyes,
Sewing by candlelight ! I hear your sighs.
But, hard as you may think your lot in life,
’Tis joy to that of the poor Sweater’s Wife,
Compelled —her social duties to fulfil
And hold her own place on the Social Hill—
To rush from rout to rout, from ball to ball,
And miss no function and no festival.
The Sweater, too, whom ye revile, is he
Not sweated also in his own degree ?
Observe him at a “Fair-trade ” Banquet rise
With brow perspiring and congested eyes,
Glass in his hand—in orator’s position—
Responding to the toast, “Free Competition ! ”
Ye wretched grumblers, by good luck debarred
From such dire ordeals —is his fate not hard ?
Ye, who can revel upon wholesome tea
And good dry bread, how would ye like to be
Condemned day after day (and not complain)
To eat roast turkey, and to drink champagne ?
While ye are toiling—all the long night through,
Perhaps—at work he’s given you to do,
Is he not lying on his bed of down,
Planning to dock your wages half-a-crown,
Manfully grappling with the problem tough
How to contrive to leave you just enough
To keep alive—he is no soulless Turk !
Yet get from you the last half-ounce of work ?
Workers ! This Pillar of your Church and State,
I beg ye do not scorn nor execrate !
Give him whate’er he asks —not much you’ll find—
He only wants the fruit—you get the rind.
The smallest sweater of them all has rights
To be considered —also appetites.
What though for bread your starving infant begs,
Remember he must have his ham and eggs !
These to refuse him would you be so base?
The knowledge that you would not lights his face,
And makes him murmur with a smile resigned :
“ There is, thank God, no hunger —l have dined.”
The sweater is no fiend, no monster fell,
Too foul for Earth, and ev’n too vile for Hell.
He is your good, kind friend. How when he dies
Some papers praise his virtues to the skies ;
Tell tearfully about the gloom outspread
The city o’er when men heard he was dead ;
Call him of Trade a Prince and Pioneer,
And end each sentence with a larger tear !
Go to the graveyard where his body lies.
Yon noble column pointing to the skies—
Seeming thereby to dumbly indicate
To all men his celestial after-fate—
Inscribed with golden letters, carven well,
Which all about his many virtues tell—
If ye but knew it, was upraised by you —
Built by your sweat, ye unbelieving crew !
Now read the last lines of his epitaph,
And if ye do not weep, pray do not laugh !
“ A just man gone to meet his blest reward—
Weep not for him. He sleepeth in the Lord.”
V. J. DALEY.
There seems to be a strange fatality about
the editorship of the Pall Mall Gazette. Since
its foundation in ’65 four of its five editors have
been fired-out by irate proprietors : Frederick
Greenwood, W. T. Stead, E. T. Cook, and Henry
Cust have all in turn departed precipitately. John
Morley, who was editor for about two years,
“resigned,” and so escaped the usual ejectment
process.
Lovely woman cropped up at Collingwood
(Melb.) police-court, the other day, in the unusual
character of an apologist for her husband’s obscene
language. Having casually assured their worships
that she had banged her old man’s head with a
boot until words unfit for publication came stream-
ing from his mouth, the gentle lady was called as
a witness for the defence. Whereupon she kissed
the Book, and deposed that she did well and truly
give “a blanky hammering” to the defendant.
For the “blanky ” she was charged with contempt,
and locked up for six hours, whilst the victim
of the assault got sentenced to 205., or three
days, on account of the original offence. Foul
language, it seems, is a misdemeanor under any
circumstances. Even though your heart’s best
love address you with a garden-shovel, or a pair of
tongs, you mustn’t reply with anything stronger
than poetry.
Of 693 students attending Melb. University
last year, 193, or two in seven, were women. Of
these women-students 48 studied in arts, 14 in
medicine and two in science ; while of 143 music
students no less than 129, or over 90 per cent.,
were women.
Argus, Age and lawyers all take unkindly
the suggestion to abolish the long vacation. Then
by all means abolish it.
When one of the directors in the late
lamented City of Melbourne Bank was asked in
court if certain securities had become valueless,
he got up on his dignity, and retorted hotly : “ 1
think it is very bad taste to ask me such questions.”
Whereupon the Argus in a burst of sarcasm :
What is the use of living in a colony possessed of the
antiquated and so-called radical notions of forty years ago,
if embodiments of Victorian radicalism are to be asked
questions devoid of good taste ?
“ Embodiments of Victorian radicalism ”! Thirty
odd members of Vic. Leg. Council are directors :
the average director is a fatted Tory, blatant in
his hatred of all forms of radicalism. But with
the Argus the man who is found out is always a
radical.
Gloucester (Eng.), which was largely
opposed to vaccination for many years, is suffer-
ing from a small pox epidemic, and a sudden
demand for lymph has sprung up in its midst.
This proves nothing, either for or against the
vaccination doctrine. Few people doubt that Dr.
Jenner discovered a means of checking the spread
of small-pox, and reducing its virulency. An in-
flamed arm is less undesirable than death, there-
fore a terror-stricken community chooses the less
evil in preference to facing the greater one with-
out taking precautions against it. The question
as to how much disease is conveyed from un-
healthy bodies to healthy bodies by compulsory
inoculation remains as vexed as ever. Likewise,
the question whether an unvaccinated person who
has survived an attack of small-pox isn’t better
off than his vaccinated brother who never caught
the infection. One thing only seems quite certain.
Vaccine is blood poison, harmless or otherwise, as
the case may be.
The Dental College and Hospital of Vic-
toria opened its new lecture-hall with an im-
posing social splash. Honorable Bob Best
made a speech, full of references to pathology,
histology, bacteriology, therapeutics and false
teeth, after which the hall was understood to be
properly started as a going concern. Otherwise,
the function consisted mainly of two simultaneous
concerts, upstairs and downstairs, varied by much
refreshments in a marquee. The Dental College,
although barely a year old, seems to be a well-
established success. Students of both sexes—
Speaker Berry’s daughter figures among the girl
graduates —are coming along steadily, and several
resident patients have testified to the quality of
the institution. Also, it has been taken up by
the Melbourne medical profession. About 70
surgeons and doctors dropped in during the other
evening to lend the light of their countenances to
Principal Oldfield, so it happened that when one
among the other 400 guests (an elderly lady) was
taken ill, she had to be fenced in with chairs to
preserve her from the awful rush of qualified
practitioners. Ultimately the patient escaped
with her life, by the mercy of Providence.
The Old Muster.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ God bless us ! it’s Joseph. How are ye, lad
how are ye ? And what have ye been doing all
these years? I'm glad to see ye, me boy.
glad to see ye. . . . Yes, I m well, thank
God, save for a fillum over me oi and a nasty, tired
feelin’ about me sometimes. How s the mother ,
all of them in the city ? . . • An the old man s
dead ? Well, well—many a post and rail i split
with him. I think I see him now. He was a
hard-working man, Joe —your father, lad ; an
clever, and honest—honest as the day. Ah, well .
it’s the will of God, Joseph—the will of God.
“ Ye’ve grown, Joseph-ye’ve grown. bhtand
up till I look at ye. Look at that now ! (to a
neighbor) the pupil’s as tall as the master- the
pupil’s as tall as the master! . . . He was
one of me first pupils —came the first day tne
school was opened, an’ a good boy he was an
clever—an’ bright. Never gave me a moment s
trouble. D’ye mind the old bark school,
Joe? Th’ oiild bark school up yonder on the
rise. I’ve got the bark yet, Joe—l’ve got tne
bark yet. I made a buggy-house out of it. Ye re
father and ye’re grandfather stripped it, and good
bark it was—like we got in the old days, x e
might hardly get a sheet now, Joe. . . And a
strong man the old man was—ye’re mothers
father, Joe—and a clever man among timber—he
hadn’t his likes in the bush. And he’s living'yet .
Ye’re grandfather, Joe ! And hearty ! Well,
well! I’m glad to hear it. There mayn tbe
another old man like him in the country. He was
a bushman o’ the old school, Joe —they’re dying
out, lad—they’re passing out—like —like th’ ould
“ The ould bark-school —15 years agone, Joseph
—l5 years. Ah, how the time goes by. The first
pupils are all getting scattered now. Yes, we’ve
got a stone school now, and a fine building it is. . .
Ah, well. D’ye mind how ye used to come on
the old grey horse, bare-back, ye and ye’re
brothers, Joe. The old horse has gone, long
ago, Joe—long ago. He used to come to the
school after ye left, and put his ould head in at
the door—many a bite of chaff I gave him. We
burnt him in the gully, Joe—the poor old horse.
. . . And ye’re dog, * Jack ’ —he lived with us
a long time, and died of old age —he died of old
age, lad.
“ I’m trying to get an exchange. I’m thinking of
getting a school somewhere down the coast. The
change would do me good. . . . No, I wouldn’t
mind leaving the old place. I’ve got some bright
scholars there now, but not like the first pupils.
Ye were one of them, Joe. Yes, I could claim a
pension now. Half-pay, Joe—half-pay. There’s
nothing to keep me nere now since the wife and
the poor old mother died. I’ve been here five-and-
thirty years, Joe —bushman, digger, farmer, and
schoolmaster. I’d like to be near the sea in my
old age. The times have changed, Joe, the times
have changed. There’s nothing to keep me here.
Come up and have tea with us, Joe —for auld lang
syne. ”
He retired next year—without a pension.
H.L.
